B004OFWLO0|2.0|I know, I know, call me a luddite. I'm perfectly okay with that title. But, these recordings, while remarkably clear and free of distracting artifacts, do not in my opinion, represent anything better than the King of the Delta Blues Singers LP's that Columbia released in 1961 and 1970, respectively. They are just \\"different\\".    While I appreciate the extras and alternates that have been found over the years, I simply can't get past the cold, clinical, & sterile sound of these \\"remastered\\" recordings. Granted they sound better than the muffled lifeless 1990 & 1996 Complete Recordings box set. But, they still lack the \\"life\\" of the old LP's. I can't speak for the sound of the original 78's as I don't own them, given their scarcity and cost these days.    In the end, I feel we are left with a revisionist view of Mr. Johnson's work. I really wanted to like this set but alas, I'll continue to enjoy my old records.

But, on the other hand, Simon has been more of a risk-taker with much less \\"humble\\" material, since he started his own solo career, and has this uncanny ability to turn apparently simple phrases, also without an apparent initial \\"sense\\" into intertwining combinations of humor, tenderness and, above all, keen observations that make every day situations or occurrences seem extraordinary. This is what a poet does. Sometimes he remembers me of Pablo Neruda, writing out of pure, pure instinct. This time he's just celebrating his maturity and sharing it with us in a totally candid and sincere way. He's not forcing anything, he's not being a prophet, he's just saying it like he sees it, at 69, and if it seems not to be profound, so be it. Maybe it's not, and this is the beauty of it. But it is not ever silly, mostly because there aren't many people who can resonate without a huge speaker, so to speak, like him.  And this is why this is no Graceland. Graceland was a great album, but it had that very conscious \\"planned\\" sense to it (not a bad thing) not unlike Sgt. Pepper. \\"So Beautiful, So What\\" is casual and relaxed but it stills managesto awaken a sense of curiosity that can only come as when one is confronted with one of those riddles that once deciphered one feels like \\"why did it take me so long to figure it out\\".     If I were to describe this work in one word, it would be \\"different\\". Plain, simply, freshly and above all, timelessly different. In a very good way.     But... it shares something with Graceland after all: it will remain playable a million times over.

Sure, there were some stand out great tracks (`The Pretender' and `Best of You' come to mind) but there was too much filler, and I wondered whether Dave and co. would ever get back to the musical heights of `The Color and the Shape'.    Even though my tastes lean more toward Blues Rock these days, I am pleased to say 'Wasting Light' blew away my expectations. There are so many great tracks it is hard to pick a stand out, and only one (Back and Forth) feels like filler. This is the first rock album in years where I can listen to it over and over and not feel tempted to skip tracks.    Openers `Bridge Burning' and `Rope' are rock at its best, with great riffs and hooks, and it's immediately apparent that the whole band are meshing and bringing their A game. The drums, the base, and of course the Foos signature wall of guitars are all at their best. Dave may not be an amazing singer, but his voice swings from melodic to dirty at all the right moments and he sounds as good as ever.    The album continues at a high standard, with anthemic `These Days' and epic `I Should Have Known' being standouts, although there is so much good material here that everyone will no doubt have their own favorite tracks. `White Limo' is a thrashing screaming rocker that reminds me of early Queens of the Stone Age and may be too balls out for some, but is in my opinion a killer track.    If I had to find something to criticize, it would be that Dave's lyrics are not especially inspired, but they're still better than 90% of what passes for rock music these days.

1400031001|5.0|The Intimate Journals of Logan Mountstuart 1906-1991 is an intermittent diary of the life of a writer and journalist, written in many different parts of the world and covering a multitude of world events. Cleverly interwoven meetings with famous people - including Hemingway, Virginia Woolf, Picasso, Martha Gellhorn and the Duke and Duchess of Windsor, with editor's footnotes, make it difficult to realise that this is the story of an entirely fictional person. We learn no more about the Duke and Duchess than we already know - however, nobody can re-write history.   Mountstuart has a huge sexual appetite, is amoral in most relationships although, after spending many months incarcerated towards the end of the war, the loss of his second wife and child causes him unimaginable anguish. The story also deals with a writer's loneliness, with his ultimate realisation that love is more life-defining than sex.     This is an entertaining, sometimes comical, book, with an inevitable sadness as we see his descent to extreme penury before being rescued by a legacy. William Boyd's writing ensures that the reader is really interested in the trivia of the everyday life of an unknown man, although certainly the singularity of that life made the story more remarkable than mine or yours would have been.     Maybe his second wife was rather conveniently removed from the scene; perhaps his flight from the results of a fling with an underage girl in New York to Nigeria was a ploy to enable Boyd to write about areas of the world with which he was familiar; but it is done with inimitable style.     It was a fascinating read, I was sorry it had to come to an end and that I had to say good-bye to an entertaining, humanly flawed character, one who became more sympathetic and kinder to others in own misfortune. Reading groups would enjoy the many aspects of this book and I guarantee that it would provoke lively discussion.

1594744769|4.0|This book was a definite surprise. It reads like a Sherman Alexie novel with its snarky narration, but also as an homage to Peter Pan and the film Nightbreed. Author Ransom Riggs has produced a fantastical tale of \\"strange\\" children, time travel, mystery, horror, and a boy's coming of age. The photos are an added bonus as they allow the reader in to this world.<br /><br />Jacob Portman's loved his grandfather's stories of an island of monsters and children with powers since he was a child. He outgrew these stories, and while eking out a living at his family's line of drug stores, his beloved grandfather is killed. Police say wild dogs, but in truth it's one of the creatures his grandpa talked about. So it comes down to this: how much of those stories was real? After sessions with a therapist, Jacob realizes the answers he's looking for are on the island his grandfather spoke of, where children can lift boulders over their heads and hover in the air, schooled and protected by a powerful headmistress. But what happens when the world outside comes in?<br /><br />MISS PEREGRINE'S is a wonderful addition to the YA fantasy genre. There's a non-traditional love triangle. There's no teenage angst. This is a pack-your-bags-and-go-on-an- adventure read. One can just sink into Jacob's thoughts: his humor, grief, joy. I especially liked his relationship with his grandpa. It was so genuine that when his grandpa dies it strikes a chord. It was a little awkward for him to start developing feelings for a certain girl at the school.<br /><br />Mr. Riggs does a fine job crafting the children. They felt real; energetic, fun loving, and full of surprises. The adults are not as genuine- they're made in a few different ways: aloof, self-interested.<br /><br />The backdrop of World World II and the photography weave through Jacob's growing up from privileged boy to a young man who's more sure of his family and where he belongs is satisfying.<br /><br />Some of the passages lose their strength because they are told as opposed to shown. Show Jacob's frustrations when he's trying to come to terms with his grandpa's death. Show his sorrow.<br /><br />Scary moments are pretty scary (at least I thought so). There is cursing and adult content. The ending eludes to a sequel. Still, a fine read. If you're up for a trip with some peculiar children, this is highly recommended.to shown. B004DMIIVO|5.0|Takashi Murakami's book Superflat presents us with deep social commentary in the guise of a discussion of the sources of current trends in anime and manga in historical Japanese art.     And what might that have to do with this movie you may ask? Because more than anything else Summer Wars is the same social commentary brought to life in anime form. It looks at the conflict Japan, and the rest of the world, faces when tradition and modern technologies and trends collide. This is made visually evident in a family, portrayed in a style that deliberately gives a the flavor of Miyazaki to things going on the the real world, while the virtual world is as deliberately Murakami in its flavor.    This sets the stage for not only a battle of visual styles, but a battle of all flavors of new and old, pitting electronic networking against old fashioned connecting in person and by phone, video games against baseball, programming against pen and paper, and in the end a contest of two wills, electronic versus human.    As such it is not only a marvel to watch, but is also an incredibly revealing and very intentional piece of social commentary where the metaphorical death of the traditional makes it clear to those affected not only how important those traditions truly are but how they must adapted by the next generation to face a new world.

B0026EWZMY|4.0|AHM was a true original which broke all the rules and represents Floyd at their quirkiest and most inventive. It's possibly Floyd's most enduring pre-DSOTM collection with a character all its own.<br /><br />The original vinyl album had a similar structure to its follow-on, `Meddle:' one side devoted to a long symphonic concept moving seamlessly through several sections (i.e. `Echoes' on `Meddle'), and the other side a collection of songs which stand well in their own right.<br /><br />The 24-minute symphonic title track owes a great deal to Kent-based avant-garde composer Ron Geesin, who at the request of Roger Waters conceived and arranged all the brass, orchestral and choral parts at the centre of the piece. Waters and Mason underpin the main theme with a slow-tempo rhythm over which Geesin's brass section lays its cowboy-western theme. The piece then moves seamlessly through several movements including a truly sublime choral section and a short funky jam from the band to return, symphonic style, to the main theme in conclusion. The whole works better than it should, and is all the more remarkable for its time in having virtually no lyrics (the choir is simply used as an instrument for sound). The result is a unique listening experience and in a way beautiful, with fine contributions from Wright and Gilmour, demonstrating here for the first time he was by now a vital member of the band with a great and maturing creative talent. A comparison with `Echoes' recorded only months later is instructive in demonstrating the evolution of ideas which led to some of Floyd's greatest musical innovations in the 1970s, when the band functioned as a productive unit working together.<br /><br />Side 2 of the vinyl album kicks off with Roger Waters' `If', an acoustic guitar piece somewhat reminiscent of `Grantchester Meadows' in musical form but with the more sinister theme of potential looming mental instability: an early indication of the direction which finally came to maturity in the underlying thematic preoccupations of DSOTM. Dave Gilmour lays over some sublime electric guitar to beef-out the final section of the song.<br /><br />Rick Wright's `Summer of 68' is possibly the best thing he ever wrote. It continues the laid-back mood with a jaunty upbeat rhythm overlaid with a fine melody supporting poignant lyrical musings about the lack of depth and meaning in an opportunistic one-night stand with a `groupie' whilst on tour: \\"I hardly even like you, I shouldn't care at all...tomorrow brings another town, another girl like you...just tell me, how do you feel?\\" The final section fills out with a blast of brass over the rhythm to echo the style of the symphonic title track.<br /><br />Dave Gilmour's `Fat Old Sun' is a slow, lazy and richly-textured song and classic Floyd, again picking up towards the end with electric guitar over Waters' and Mason's slow insistent rhythm section. Nice.<br /><br />The closer `Alan's Psychedelic Breakfast' has been described by members of the band in later years as `absolute rubbish.' This is too harsh: it's interesting and different, and at the time of first release caused much amusement in the listening audience (I know - I was there). It's a studio piece in three musical sections, formed around short episodes voiced by Alan (one of Floyd's road crew) as he prepares his breakfast complete with sound effects, following a night of - we can only imagine - some psychedelic mind excursion courtesy of exotic chemical substances. It's a somewhat downbeat closer and interesting in its historic context in that it illustrates the experimental and think-out-of-the-box mind set of Floyd at the time, before they settled on projects of themed songwriting which led to the greater commercial success of the DSOTM and WYWH period.<br /><br />AHM is a fine album and still, after 40 years, eminently listenable with some great music. It has a more mellow feel than some of Floyd's other offerings and hard-driving rock numbersare absent, but it's none the worst for that and is, at heart, quality stuff.<br /><br />The album's title was, reportedly, taken from a newspaper headline about a woman being fitted with a new kind of hi-tech pacemaker, spontaneously chosen by Roger Waters and with no direct relevance to the content of the music. It was a good choice, though. The improbable cover photo of the cow was, of course, a stroke of genius and forever marks the album out as distinctive and immediately recognisable.<br /><br />My copy dates from 1971 or thereabouts, when the album was still riding high in the UK album charts (it had been Floyd's first number one). It's always had a 'Side One' label on BOTH sides: I used to think this might be standard, but later discovered it was an error confined to a very few examples. If you find one of these, you'll have a rarity. You can tell the difference between the two sides only by the darker track separator ring after 'If' on Side Two.are absent, but it's none the worst for that and is, at heart, quality stuff.<br /><br />The album's title was, reportedly, taken from a newspaper headline about a woman being fitted with a new kind of hi-tech pacemaker, spontaneously chosen by Roger Waters and with no direct relevance to the content of the music. It was a good choice, though. The improbable cover photo of the cow was, of course, a stroke of genius and forever marks the album out as distinctive and immediately recognisable.<br /><br />My copy dates from 1971 or thereabouts, when the album was still riding high in the UK album charts (it had been Floyd's first number one). It's always had a 'Side One' label on BOTH sides: I used to think this might be standard, but later discovered it was an error confined to a very few examples. If you find one of these, you'll have a rarity. You can tell the difference between the two sides only by the darker track separator ring after 'If' on Side Two.

0811876373|5.0|I love this book! I love cake pops, not so much making them. They are much harder work than I thought, or maybe I'm just lazy. I made twelve and had to stop. here's a few things I learned that are not in the book.<br /><br />You can use peanut butter, cream cheese.....just about anything sticky to bind the cake. This way you can adjust the sweetness of the filling. If you use pure peanut butter.....your pop may come out saltier than you would like, so test it out first. (I kind of liked mine a little saltier, my cake pop ended up tasting like reese's pieces). Or do half peanut butter, half frosting or whatever. My next idea is make chocolate ganache and use that as a binder. just melt chocolate chips, cream (or milk) and butter, stir until smooth and add to crumbled cake. (I detest store bought frosting).<br /><br />I made two cakes, one boxed strawberry and one boxed chocolate. The strawberry cake was so moist that when I put only 1/2 cup of frosting it was extremely mushy and hard to place on a stick, even when chilled. I didn't have any extra cake on hand so I crumbled up some bread and added it to the mix to dry it up, worked great.<br /><br />The chocolate cake however, was a tad dryer and it came out perfect. So the point is to start with just a little frosting. Good luck and have fun! After I made the cake pops I couldn't stand even to look at anything sweet. They tasted better the next day.

0525422943|4.0|A year and a half ago I read a book called \\"If I Stay\\" by Gayle Foreman. Cherish the ones that you love, hold them dear and not take advantage of their existence, because you never know when that predicted existence may be quickly taken from you.<br /><br />\\"If I Stay\\" (though abruptly finished) was a complete story, so when I found out that Ms. Foreman decided to write a follow-up I was confused. After being handing marching orders by Mia, Adam is forced to face his harsh (tabloid driven) life without the comfort of the only person who understands him, and being uncertain as to how to handle things, he resorts to bitterness and booze. With his world crumbling around him, and memories flooding his every thought, Adam decides to take a break. Just a quick one really, a walk through the park, but when he stumbles upon a flyer announcing Mia's next concert he suddenly realizes his past will forever haunt his future unless he confronts it. With only one night left ahead of him to make amends or set her free for good Adam goes for it. Will Mia ever fully understand what she did to him? The reason she chose to return to her life. So returning to the story three years later only to find out that Adam and Mia are no longer together was (in some parts) physically painful. The story (like the first) was written with a handful of flashbacks, but the means of communication between the two were drastically different. While Mia is a musician, she is not a songwriter like Adam, and when Foreman chose to change her lead character, the ability witness pain through lyrics, and poetic inner dialogue suddenly became available. It is important to note (though my love for the book is telling me not to) that the story is a bit slow in places, and I think there was a perfect moment (about 1 chapter from the end) that would have served as a better ending, but overall...a very good read for someone who doesn't mind emotional journeys.<br /><br />Happy Reading my fellow Kindle-ites and remember: Life is too short to be saddled with half truths. 0783225547|4.0|I liked Dante's Peak. This is the only 1990s volcano disaster movie that matters. It is well written, directed, and accurate.    The elements of suspense and surprise are maintained through the entire movie. The name of the town is foreboding. One gets the feeling as the movie progresses that the actors are proceeding through various torturous punishments of Dante Alighieri's Inferno. The characters experience real injuries and real human suffering. The shocking first scene drew me in immediately and set the tone for the entire movie.    Dante's Peak is a classic doomsayer-gets-it-right tale. Pierce Brosnan brings just the right personality to that role. While it is true that the USGS might have had sufficient information to sound the alarm even in the 1990s, if a team of USGS-sponsored geeks had alerted the mayor, he would have listened, none of the events in the movie would have taken place, and the movie would be called \\"The Orderly Evacuation and Rebuilding of Dante's Peak\\". Bo-ring!     Factual analysis: The portrayal of a volcanic eruption in the Cascades and its effects on the landscape is shockingly realistic. The special effects, pyroclastic eruptions, corrosive sulfuric acid mudflows, tephra and explosive eruptions, are very well done, and most occur in the proper sequence. The only part that required much suspension of disbelief is the scene where the hero's SUV gets stuck in a pahoehoe (Hawaiian, not Cascadian) lava flow and miraculously escapes without even a flat tire. I suppose that was inserted over the protests of the scientific advisers (and common sense) due to the need for \\"edge of the seat\\" suspense. This is an action movie, after all.    Overall, I thought the movie was very well made and directed, and I appreciate the fact that the writers insisted on realism over Hollywood spectacle (although in this movie, the realistic parts are spectacular indeed).

B00005R8GU|5.0|The backstory: Marshall Chess was licking his wounds over the savaging he took from the neo-psychedelicised albums he'd produced notoriously for Muddy Waters and Howlin' Wolf. Mike Bloomfield, a friend from high school and a musician who'd learned most of his blues at the feet and the sides of Waters and the other Chicago blues masters, suggested to the youngest Chess that he think of an album on which Waters would be supported by the younger blues revivalists who'd learned from or been inspired by him otherwise.    To his eternal credit, Chess ran with the idea. He saw and raised Bloomfield, who'd first made his bones and his reputation by wheedling his way into jamming with Muddy and the like in his late, audacious, and troubled teens. He enlisted Stax stalwart Duck Dunn to play bass; Sam Lay, Bloomfield's old running mate in the Butterfield Blues Band and Howlin' Wolf's former stage drummer, and then playing in the Waters band after recuperating from the illness that took him out of the Butterfield lineup; Buddy Miles, then running his Buddy Miles Express (out of the remnant of the ill-fated Electric Flag) on Mercury; Paul Butterfield himself; and, Otis Spann, Muddy's longtime keyboard stalwart now launched on his own tragically short-lived career (he would die of cancer in 1970), who ditched a New York club engagement to do this project.    The idea was to record the sessions around Bloomfield and Butterfield's plans for a Chicago charity performance in April 1969, with Marshall Chess and another high school running mate of his and Bloomfield's, the producer Norman Dayron, picking the right material out of the Waters vaults for these young turks to cut with the masters. They even hunted down (probably at Bloomfield's suggestion, and who's to say he didn't contribute from his own stock of the stuff) out vintage Fender amplifiers. Then, he kept the champagne flowing for Muddy and the beers flowing for the young turks, rounded them into new recording facilities on21st Street, and let them cut loose.     The purists still smirk over whether the young turks were intimidated into submission, but what actually transpires is young sons paying the daddies who taught them their blues respect without lapsing into obsequiousness. Bloomfield may be somewhat restrained in many turns, but in others he lets just the right amount loose to wrap his elders without smothering them. Likewise for Butterfield, who shines precisely by reining himself in at the precise strategic moments. Anchored by the Lay-Dunn rhythm section (Miles doesn't show up until a portion of the concert that produced the live sides), with Dunn obviously thrilled to join up to one of the Chicago blues legends (Lay's almost freewheeling approach must have been a huge vacation for a Dunn who was accustomed to Al Jackson, Jr.'s tautly deep groove), Waters is more relaxed with his blues than he seems to have been for too many years, and Spann as usual is a model of self-assurance over the piano, playing almost as though they were younger, fresher, and making the best of what they had in the old Chess studios.    Maybe the best exercise of the studio portion is \\"You Can't Lose What You Ain't Never Had,\\" originally a haunting single for Waters in 1964 but now a deeper, more harrowing performance precisely because these are men, young and old, steeped in its message and barely able to escape it no matter how enthusiastically they play it. Bloomfield unfurls perhaps the most wrenching solo of his time on the project; Butterfield blows as though crying up from a grave; and Lay's drumming could be mistaken for the Grim Reaper having a perverse laugh as he stomps across the boneyard, while Waters sings it as though he's only wrapped its implications for the first time, looked the bastard in the eye, and fought him to a draw. But if you need other references to begin, go no further than the set opener, a remake of \\"All Aboard\\" (itself Muddy's original re-imaginging of \\"Mystery Train\\") that chugs so furiously yet atmospherically it damn near leaves Muddy's original on the platform, particularly buttressed by a very sensitive harmonica support from Butterfield.    The concert sides are exuberant, celebratory, yet sober, each musician staying within himself, the whole becoming something almost greater than the sum of its parts. Marshall Chess's own epitaph is a better review than I could offer here: \\"The sound of five thousand kids singing 'I've Got My Mojo Working' is the most powerful thing I've ever heard,\\" he said of the concert side closer, in two parts, Miles coming in to play part two, and the entire ensemble driving the chestnut so far home---Spann particularly is working the pistons as though they had his name on them---that if you blink you see only the exhaust of the motors.    It's as if Muddy Waters took it for precisely the career reviver it almost was for him. It became the best-selling recording of his career to date and, while he had a few innings still to play before Chess collapsed entirely as a working label, it proved to be the pilot fish for what made his real career revival---in Johnny Winter's respectful hands---erupt for keeps in 1978, with \\"Hard Again.\\" As with \\"Fathers & Sons,\\" Waters in 1978 went back to his absolute basics, with likewise sympatico musicians, and enjoyed best-selling albums and big concert rewards for the rest of his life, and it was only his due.    But it is only fair to say the seed was planted, with the album he cut with the men he taught explicitly and often directly, when Mike Bloomfield's almost offhand suggestion to his old high school buddy turned into a working project and, in time, a best-selling album that just might be the last of the classic Chess blues gasps. Howlin' Wolf himself would get very close to this mojo with his \\"London Howlin' Wolf Sessions\\" (with Eric Clapton, Steve Winwood, Bill Wyman, and Charlie Watts anchoring the proceedings), but close isn't paydirt. None of the blues supersessions to follow in the years to come---not Wolf or Muddy's London exercises; not Albert King's noble exercise with Stevie Ray Vaughan; not the Albert Collins-Johnny Copeland-Robert Cray goonbash; not even \\"Riding with the King,\\" B.B. King's loving exercise with Eric Clapton---have ever equaled \\"Fathers & Sons\\" for the pleasure and the revelation of revisiting the blues with deep feeling and unbridled joy.

B004IQMXD4|5.0|I have to tell you right up front that I am completely enamored by this record. I did not expect to be and did not want to start this review off with a negative so there you have it. Enamored.<br /><br />I never thought a whole lot of Stevie Nicks' solo career. She was the force behind the last evolution of Fleetwood Mac and she deserves a place of fame for that. But I always felt she did not stand too well on her own - almost as if she needed a strong supporting cast to make it work. That plus the whole mystical witch thing and I figured it was mostly music for women who read bad novels about unicorns and vampires. But, like most men of my era, I could not look past her sensual warble and overall hotness so I gave her plenty of room.<br /><br />I bought In Your Dreams almost as a novelty. When an aging and prominent rocker of my era lays out a new disc it's worth seeing what they are up to. I have done it many times in the past few years. But I was not prepared for the tour de force that is Stevie Nicks' new record.<br /><br />The album lights off with a commanding establishment of Stevie's still-strong vocal chops and her surprisingly non-mystical writing. Secret Love expresses everyone's desire - love, or at least some semblance of it:<br /><br />I am not asking for forever from you<br />I'm just asking to be held for awhile<br /><br />Lyrically this song demands some examination from anyone on the path of life and love. And sonically Stevie's voice is strong and sure, with that same quality we used to love but perhaps tempered or matured. This song is all about that timeless search and is surprisingly full of profundity:<br /><br />I am not asking for salvation from you<br />I'm just asking to be saved for awhile.<br /><br />That song alone has more content and passion than the last dozen albums by name-your-favorite-vacuous-pop-star. And we're just getting started.<br /><br />For What It's Worth comes across to me as a study in Stevie's history and life in Rock & Roll - \\"I got to sing, I got dance\\". Stevie's lovely warble shines to perfection here as she talks of life and love and loss. She sings as someone who knows the desire and goal of life is to love but it doesn't always work out that way - \\"We knew it couldn't last, and that was hard\\". She recognizes that life isn't a fairy tale. But Ms. Nicks had the life of a rock goddess and music was her true love, her constant, her destiny. A sad and beautiful song.<br /><br />In Your Dreams sweeps away the melancholy and brings us a high-energy song that would have fit the peak era of Fleetwood Mac. We are immediately sent guessing - who is she singing about. I will tell you my theory in a minute. The bottom line here is that all that pain and tears aren't necessary - because \\"it's a beautiful day.\\" And a fine segue into Wide Sargasso Sea. I can't help but be firmly convinced that this is about her relationship with Mick Fleetwood back-in-the-day. Lots of passion here and allusions to burning down his house while escaping herself. Hmmm.<br /><br />Next up is a song about New Orleans. What is it about N.O.? I have never been but people get impassioned when they sing about it. Stevie makes me almost want to go and see for myself. And clever and deep writing makes it more compelling:<br /><br />In the midst of the sea of dreams<br />Lies a perfect storm<br />In a sea of tears<br />Lies a city ghost<br /><br />Throw in some Anne Rice and vampire references and it is pretty interesting writing. Plus the music is simply gorgeous and her vocal hooks compelling.<br /><br />These next two look like they take us down that Welsh witch path to loony mysticism that causes me to fear her music. But Moonlight is actually an old song revisited and appears to be largely autobiographical. It certainly describes her past and her loves and carries a definite Fleetwood Mac tone to boot. She claims to have paid a big price for the life she has lived and she creates a twilight world of rock and roll genius examining what was and what could never be. This song almost hurts lyrically. It is the perfect set up for Annabel Lee, a Nicks / Edgar Allan Poe collaboration that makes perfect sense. Stevie Nicks is probably the only Rock & Roll diva who could pull this off:<br /><br />But our love was stronger by far than the love<br />Of those who were older than we<br />Of many far wiser than we<br />And neither the angels in heaven above<br />Nor the demons down under the sea<br />Can ever dissever my soul from the soul<br />Of the beautiful Annabel Lee.<br /><br />I hear some wishful thinking in there and this song is sweeping and majestic and romantic.<br /><br />Soldier's Angel was challenging for me because it seems so out of place. It has a very heavy handed Lindsey Buckingham vibe to it, not a bad thing, but a little too dark and morose for the rest of the proceedings. But apparently Stevie has done some work with war vets in D.C. and I think this song illuminates that idea that we are all connected to war. None of us are left out of the battles as a spouse or parent or lover or just friend and we all pay a price. We are all at war - a thought worth advancing in a culture that doesn't understand that concept.<br /><br />The next three are continued reflections on Stevie's life and loves in the rock & roll world of fame and fortune. Everybody Loves You means what it says. Ghosts Are Gone sounds like it came from a Country & Western bar and is packed with ideas of revelation and resolution on what is gone. And You May Be The One is a gorgeous blues number that features the absolutely heavenly backup vocals of Sharon Celani and Lori Nicks (who you can hear sprinkled throughout this record).<br /><br />Italian Summer is a lush romantic memory of a more-than-pleasant experience. We have all had these times that stick in our minds - one of mine is ten days in March in 2008 in Monterey, and all the memories and thoughts are even and pleasant. This song carries those ideas in simple phrases like \\"you put down that guitar and handed me a rose\\". The message is strong and clear of deep romance and warm melancholy.<br /><br />The disc finishes up with Cheaper Than Free and it's almost too much. But it is a sweet song especially after all the above and asks important questions like:<br /><br />What does money not buy?<br />You and I.<br /><br />Grammatically incorrect but simply and remarkably true. This song sums up that it is brighter, warmer, better, deeper and more exciting, this life and love that we find along the way. A beautiful song, sung as a duet with Dave Stewart, and in the hands of a young pop star it would have fallen flat. But it rings true considering the source, well-versed in the ways of life.<br /><br />And that is really where this album shines. It is not a caricature of an aging pop star. It is strong and sure, vulnerable but wise. And not at all mystical but real and practical while being shockingly romantic. Stevie Nicks is not singing from songs written for her. She is singing 62 years of life & love & regrets & passion & pain. And you get to hear it, feel it, experience it with her. You can relate to some or all of it because it is real.<br /><br />And I have to throw in this idea I had while I listened to this repeatedly in my car on a trip without benefit of album notes or the internet. I felt that this album was largely a reflection on one of rock's great love stories - Stevie Nicks and Lindsey Buckingham. And in another universe and another time, maybe one filled with moonbeams and unicorns, those two would have married and lived a life in the suburbs while raising a family, blissfully in love. But the world of fame is hard on romance.<br /><br />There is nothing cool or cutting edge or even edgy about this record. It is simply beautiful and majestic and right. If you have lived and loved you can take part in Stevie Nicks' musical and lyrical journey, the album of a lifetime.<br /><br />Bravo, Bella Donna. Bravo.Free and it's almost too much. But it is a sweet song especially after all the above and asks important questions like:    What does money not buy?  You and I.    Grammatically incorrect but simply and remarkably true. This song sums up that it is brighter, warmer, better, deeper and more exciting, this life and love that we find along the way. And that is really where this album shines. It is not a caricature of an aging pop star. It is strong and sure, vulnerable but wise. And not at all mystical but real and practical while being shockingly romantic. Stevie Nicks is not singing from songs written for her. She is singing 62 years of life & love & regrets & passion & pain. And you get to hear it, feel it, experience it with her. You can relate to some or all of it because it is real.    And I have to throw in this idea I had while I listened to this repeatedly in my car on a trip without benefit of album notes or the internet. 1439107955|5.0|There are some important limitations of this book, but first my thoughts on why it is so good, and who is the targeted reader.    There are a number of chronological threads comprising the book, each looks at a particular problem in devising an effective treatment. These threads have real patients to be treated and one does get real feeling for them. They are not just props. However the meat of the book is in the scientific work needed to better understand the nature of the cancer as well as the chemotherapy regimens which could make use of that understanding. So the reader should be one of an inquisitive mind who has true interest in science and medicine. Other readers can skip it.    The biggest plus for me was the consistent effort by the author to keep the science limited to what is important and then making every effort to ensure that the reader understands clearly. One sees that the author doesn't want to write, he wants to communicate. And I found myself engrossed in the stories being told along with understanding what had not been clear to me previously. Furthermore, the research is being undertaken by real people whose situations we come to learn about and appreciate.    On the limitation side, I don't think this qualifies as a broad \\"biography of cancer\\". The focus is completely on finding treatments and cures, nothing on prevention. The treatments are all in the chemotherapy realm except for one excellent part on the surgery that has been used for breast cancer. Almost nothing on the radiation side or surgeries for other cancers. There is one brief but good excursion into understanding how lung cancer develops. As far as the chemotherapy, it is almost all related to cancer of the blood (leukemia) or breast cancer. But if one has particular interest in prostate cancer or colon cancer or bladder cancer or numerous other common cancers one might be disappointed by this book.    But this is an excellent book on the subjects that the author undoubtedly knows best. One shouldn't fault a book for what wasn't included, I don't think. And of course different cancers do have much in common.

B00007FOMP|5.0|Brilliance many times comes out of pain and a tortured soul, never more has this statement been true than when describing this artist and this wonderfully historic album.  WHAT'S GOING ON is the album Marvin had wanted to make for a very long time but was held back from making due the strictness of Motown's founder and president, Berry Gordy.  Marvin, who went through many trauma's as a child and young adult with an alcoholic & abusive father as well as a marriage that was beginning to fall apart, had a lot of emotions bottled up inside.<br /><br />He truly needed an outlet and wasn't being given the freedom to do so musically which is one of the reasons I believe he turned so heavily to drugs early on.  It was through a series of letters he received from his brother who was serving in Vietnam that this incredible album and it's overall message would come into being.  His brother was informing him about the war and what that all entailed and Marvin begin to tie that in with life here in the U.S. and what it was going to be like for his brother, or anyone in his situation, to return home.  And from that, WHAT'S GOING ON was conceived and born.<br /><br />It truly is a tortured soul, begging, no, SCREAMING for all to come together and put aside petty bigotry, hate and war.  But it's also, in many ways, a confessional for Marvin as, if you pay close attention, he is revealing many of the thoughts and struggles he's personally dealing with at the time.  It's Marvin's life struggles, the \\"microcosm\\" relaying the overall issues and struggles facing the nation and world as a whole, the \\"macrocosm.\\"  It's an album that really and truly needs to be listened to all at once as it truly was meant to be a continuous message and not a series of \\"singles.\\"<br /><br />We've heard of \\"concept\\" albums before but this was the truest since of that term as each song is a part of an overall story that Mr. Gaye takes us on.  With each note he sings, you can hear the plea in the man's voice calling out for both outward togetherness and inner peace.  He was actually contemplating leaving music behind altogether prior to the creation of this album.  However, his brothers stories plus his own personal demons, compelled him both to write & record this album as well as fight Gordy and the powers that be at Motown for it's promotion and release.  It's amazing to listen to Gordy now as he reflects back on what he told Marvin about the album then versus the conversations they had in the years after it's release and historical success.<br /><br />I may never have been as moved as when I first heard this album for the first time, the tears flowed, I danced, shouted, cried out and expressed just about every kind of emotion humanly possible.  I truly love this album and pray that this brilliant, loving, tortured soul has finally come to know the peace he agonized over and sang about to us on this record so long ago.  This is a quintessential album and EVERY music lover no matter what genre you may prefer should give it a listen.  I promise you will not be disappointed.<br /><br />There was never an album recorded like this one up to that point, it truly was/is one of a kind as was/is it's creator.  Marvin Gaye was a true musical giant and deeply profound man, his work is being reassessed more and more as it should be as some of the very best ever recorded.  While my personal favorite album of his is HERE MY DEAR due to it's extremely personal nature, this is, without question, his most influential and historic album worthy of any and all conversations about the greatest recordings of all time.e calling out for both outward togetherness and inner peace. 0345522478|3.0|Our hero, Atticus O'Sullivan, is a 2000-year -old Druid currently living in Arizona. He has a sword that some of the Tuatha de Danann think he should return to them. He runs a New Age bookstore, and looks like he's in his early twenties.     Like most urban fantasies, this one has our hero talking in the first person, as a kind of running commentary on his life. It starts with a battle outside his bookstore, in which we learn of Atticus's powers, and something of the forces arrayed against him.     This book has a lot going for it. It is aptly named - he is being pursued relentlessly, his lawyer is a werewolf, he can shapeshift into a wolfhound, and his companion Irish Wolfhound, Oberon, is a distinctive, original character. The Irish pantheon is nicely drawn, and like Neil Gaimon's American Gods, they are not the only supernatural beings in this universe. There are gods from all over, and Coyote laughs when things do not go as Atticus might wish. There is a lot of action. There is even a reference to the SCA as a cover for talking about historical-sounding feuds.     But there are drawbacks, too. The narrative was not compelling. Everyone had motivations that read like D&D game (or current equivalent) - one layer deep. The action was too much telling, not showing. As the action got hotter, the narrator still talked in long complex sentences that slowed down the story. For instance, in the climactic battle, Atticus says, \\"You've hounded me for centuries. And you might have hounded me for many more, but your petty jealously of Brighid has brought you to this end!\\" The dialog is not bad, per se, but it does not read like something one would actually say in the midst of a sword fight. His words were not a taunt - they were a summation for the reader.    A story gains energy from the introduction of new characters. But the author constantly introduced new characters, each with a backstory, so there's constant exposition. Before the character is fully absorbed, then hisor her moment on the stage was over, and someone new shows up.     The story was eventually all tied up, with just enough loose ends to leave room for a sequel. I might even read it. The series has potential, and room for growth.

The movie was nominated for one Oscar (best score) and contains a number of songs, as well as having a strong musical presence throughout.<br /><br />Watching a classic Disney title is like stepping back in time. Compared to modern animation, the classics contain more warmth and less peril. There's also considerably less action. The stories are full of wonder and revel in showing the viewer the setting. One common theme running through all of the titles is the friendly nature of animals and inanimate objects.<br /><br />In Alice in Wonderland, all of the animals talk. You'll also hear from flowers and a door knob. It's a strange fantasy world inhabited by weird creatures. The adventure begins when Alice follows a white rabbit down a hole. She stumbles across such creatures as oysters, a walrus, the Mad Hatter, the Cheshire Cat and an army of somewhat threatening playing cards.<br /><br />Alice can change size by eating things in Wonderland. This can be helpful, but regularly causes her problems. Nothing is logical because it's the stuff of dreams. Most of the creatures are friendly and well-meaning, but there are a few moments of danger when Alice encounters the Queen of Hearts.<br /><br />The movie has a few too many songs for my particular taste, although it works perfectly as a family film. It's not hard to see why the movie has enchanted children for the past 60 years as there's always something interesting happening. For adults watching it alone, it won't appeal to everyone in the way that perhaps a Pixar movie would. This is mainly aimed at children.<br /><br />I found myself recognizing some of the voices. The Cheshire Cat was voiced by Sterling Holloway who also played Winnie the Pooh and Kaa the Snake in The Jungle Book. Kathryn Beaumont, who is still alive, voiced Wendy Darling in Peter Pan.<br /><br />While the movie is a lot of fun, it lacks the heart and innocence of other early Disney classics such as Bambi or Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs. That said, it deserves a place in your library if you have young children. The colors, songs, memorable creatures and fast pace will hold the attention of most children.<br /><br />Video Quality 5/5<br />Quite simply, the transfer couldn't be better. The colors are bright and the image is clean. It looks like the characters were drawn yesterday. The only thing that makes the movie look dated is the style of the animation. It's a lot flatter and lacks the three-dimensional appearance of modern Disney movies such as Bolt or the Pixar titles. Nobody will be disappointed with the appearance of this 60th anniversary Blu-ray edition.<br /><br />Audio Quality 4.5/5<br />There are two English audio tracks included, along with French and Spanish tracks. One English track is a restored stereo version of the original soundtrack. My preferred version is the lossless DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 mix. Everything is clear, but the track is front-heavy. You will hear something from your rears, but it won't match more recent releases. However, this is another fine upgrade over previous releases.<br /><br />Special Features 5/5<br /><br />There are over four hours of additional features, the best of which is the 76-minute Through the Keyhole: A Companion's Guide toWonderland feature.<br /><br />Deleted Materials includes 21 minutes of footage that didn't make it into the film.<br /><br />The remaining features include a song, an interactive game, TV specials and reflections on the film.<br /><br />The Alice in Wonderland Blu-ray offers a huge jump in quality over previous releases of the film. Firstly conflicting interests in more ambitious more publicity driven endeavors through the space race with Russia and the endeavors through Disneyland make them invest only so much in this production. What they do invest essentially does not flesh out the whole film it is uneven and choppy due to conflicting artistic interests. Firstly they want a naive sixteen year old princess but the angular style reminiscent of UPA and Warner Bros. in order to stylistically change their direction. The stylistic change prohibits such a design of a juvenile naive sixteen year old virgin, as she looks more mature and aware and developed than this. Because Walt wanted both he essentially sacrifices and endurable princess character for a style that differs from past Disney endeavors which although distinct character development suffers. Again she is developed to a completely different direction and is not sympathetic because her awareness makes it seem that all of her hardships she brings upon herself.     Further more another problem with this project is that the only really well fleshed out character is Eleanor Audley's Maleficent. The voice delivery for her is perfect and is an exceptional expansion on what made the Snow White Queen so endurable. However this film becomes more of a character study and vehicle for her and is told from her perspective so I laugh at the idea that they are trying to make a new spin on Sleeping Beauty with Tim Burton when this concept was essentially executed in the original. They neglect to flesh out the other characters which makes this film depressing seeing her as the only compelling character. The only reason you empathize with the cast is because you share a common enemy in Maleficent she is malevolence taken to its extremes in a fictional sense. She seemsto intimidate and scare people through her firmness and seductive appearance initially as she is to frail to look as if she is any physical threat. Though when it comes to character study as we notice as the film progresses she becomes more erratic her physical appearance deteriorates in the sixteen years she searches for Aurora. Character Study is so effective for her exclusively that she is the only one who really progresses throughout the film. The whole Sixteen Year old concept was used in much greater effect with Snow White but probably at its zenith with Glen Keane's Aeriel in The Little Mermaid. The two female leads seem to be based on Hollywood Personalities but in a renaissance historical context as artistically much of the visuals are based. They are so focused on technical proficiency in this film namely to exploit the new technology of Technarama and with Eyvind Earle they took great advantage of the technology through intricate surroundings and atmosphere with some influence of minimalism as well.     The reason why this is not Disney's zenith is first off I could cite better character development in other films perhaps with the exception of a more compelling villain, Chernonbog's appearance in scale and might could be debatable. So really had this been greater it would have had the same effect that Snow White or other Disney classics did in the sense that it brings back interest in theatrical animation when the industry was transitioning to television. It did not such thing as many of the laid-off artists found their way to Hanna Barabera who many of the big suits at Disney ironically didn't consider them much competition, yet some of the most proficient artists in this film found their way there shortly after.     This was probably Walt's wake up call the man who was synonymous with fiscal irresponsibility with Roy keeping him in line finally conceded that Roy was right. It was a good run but following this they cease producing epics arguably until the renaissance. Overall this film has just as great use of perspective and scale and versatile use of color keys. Though visual beauty does not prevent under-developed characters. Which again the biggest pro in this film is its distinct visual style.

I was initially turned off a bit by the childish world of the lyrics -- I could certainly understand why the band's style was referred to \\"twee rock\\" by a sceptical portion of the musical press. Belle and Sebastian frontman Stuart Murdoch was nearly 30 years old when TIGERMILK was recorded, but his lyrics are very often centered around school and the travails of being a teenager.<br /><br />But, TIGERMILK eventually won me over completely. On closer examination, the lyrics hint at darker currents and are not mere childhood nostaglia. The pop hooks are irrestible, but Murdoch's logorrhea delivery makes each song a meaty story and nothing is mere fluff. Touches of trumpet and careful production enrich the DIY feel of guitars and drums. Another strong point of TIGERMILK is that it was originally a lark for the band members (only 1000 copies were initially pressed) and the album includes a few pleasant facets that might have been smoothed over by a producer too focused on creating the perfect product. The keyboard-heavy \\"Electronic Renaissance\\" doesn't jive with everything else, but it's enjoyable and its presence confers an air of authenticity.<br /><br />As spring has finally come to my corner of the world, TIGERMILK is a record I can't get enough of. I look forward to exploring the rest of Belle and Sebastian's output.

B003KGBIRK|4.0|David Cronenberg's \\"Videodrome\\" has become something of a cult classic since it was released in the 1982. Like many of Cronenberg's other films, \\"Videodrome\\" focuses on a virus of sorts that changes humanity--in this case, though, its not a modified rabis virus, or mutated form of VD (or even the \\"mind as virus\\" as in \\"Scanners\\")but, instead, on the media as virus altering us by altering our perception of reality.<br /><br />The Criterion Blu-ray is pulled from the same high definition source as the DVD released a couple of years back looks marvelous but newly transferred for Blu-ray. \\"Videdrome\\" was made at a time when film stocks were going through transition (and like many 80's films)looks a bit vibrant than we might expect a film made today to look. Criterion has done a superb job of creating the ultimate edition of this film. The film looks nearly flawless with no noticeable print damage, speckles evident in other DVD editions (except the Criterion of course) cleaned up. There's a nice, pleasing noticeable level of fine grain and I doubt the film has ever looked this good--including the original prints that ran when the movie was in theaters back in the day.<br /><br />The print is the version that Cronenberg submitted to the MPAA prior to additional trimming to bring the film in line to what the MPAA considered an \\"R\\" rated film. That's important because the MPAA insisted that one key sequence in the film be altered to gain its \\"R\\" rating along with a number of other minor trims.<br /><br />The audio soundtrack sounds extremely good with a 24 bit transfer. Dialogue is crisp and clear and Howard Shore's creepy sythesizer based score sounds marvelous here. It's a pity that the original sound elements didn't under go a 5.1 or stereo mix for this edition but what we do get sounds terrific.<br /><br /> We get two audio commentaries--the first features Cronenberg and his DP Mark Irwin discussing everything from the themes of the film and how the photography embodies them to technical challenges they faced in shooting the film. The second features actor Woods and Deborah Harry who played Renn's lover Nicki in the film. Woods is quite talkative providing considerable insight into his role in the film and what attracted him to working with Cronenberg.<br /><br />We also get the 2000 short film \\"Camera\\" which was produced to celebrate the anniversary of the Toronto Film Festival. It compliments \\"Videodrome\\" perfectly almost like an extension of some of the themes from the film. Running around 6 minutes the film was shot on video except for the very last shot.<br /><br />We also get a very good documentary \\"Forging the New Flesh\\" that examines \\"Videodrome\\" within the context of its time and its relevance today. There's also \\"Effects Men\\" an audio interview as well as the complete footage from the Samurai dream sequence.<br /><br />Rounting out the special features is the original promotional featurette, theatrical trailer, a roundtable discussion entitled \\"Fear on Film\\" from 1982 featuring Cronenberg as well as behind-the-scenes photos. As with all later Criterion releases we get a booklet with essays analyzing the film.<br /><br />While this is expensive compared to the regular DVD edition, the Criterion Collection edition of \\"Videodrome\\" has the film looking the best it ever has with a recent high definition cleaned up transfer as well as extension supplements on the making of the film. For those interested there follows a summary of the plot--<br /><br />Up and coming media mogul Max Renn (James Woods) is looking for the next big thing to attract an audience to his small cable TV station channel 83. He thinks he has found it with the odd almost hallucinagenic pirate TV program \\"Videodrome\\" where production values and story are jetisoned for raw, brutal torture and violence committed against the average person. What Renn uncovers though is a conspriacy to alter humanity as those who watch \\"Videodrome\\" begin to have waking halluncinations of a bizarre variety; TVs come to life becoming an extension of human beings; a gun grows into his hand becoming an extension of it; variations of the things he sees on \\"Videodrome\\" appear in Renn's world but only to him.<br /><br />Fans of the movie know the rest of the important plot points and discussing anything else would be major spoilers but if you've seen any of Cronenberg's other films you'll get a sense of the general direction of THIS film.<br /><br />Recommended.n of human beings; a gun grows into his hand becoming an extension of it; variations of the things he sees on \\"Videodrome\\" appear in Renn's world but only to him. B00005JMCN|4.0|\\"Seabiscuit,\\" writer-director Gary Ross's film of Laura Hillenbrand's beloved biography of the Depression-era champion racehorse and the men who owned and trained him, is far from a perfect film. Scene after scene is bathed in the golden light of early morning or late afternoon. It's got that stately pace, fanatic attention to period detail, sweeping score and overweening prettiness that beg for Oscar nominations. Because Ross is trying to reach a broad audience, his script frequently trips over itself trying to educate viewers about a part of history (the Great Depression) and a sport (horse racing) with which many of us have lost connection. I did not care for Tobey Maguire's portrayal of Seabiscuit's jockey, Red Pollard, which struck me as self-pitying rather than the tough, funny, desperate character of the book. In scenes where the characters are supposedly interrupting each other, the interruptions sound contrived. Fans of Hillenbrand's book will catch and perhaps call Ross out on the way he condenses and eliminates facts to serve his purposes. He clearly wants to tell the inspiring story of Seabiscuit, but he also wants to contrast the \\"we're all in this together\\" helping hand approach of FDR's New Deal with the \\"you're on your own\\" attitude and ever-widening gap between the wealthy and the rest of us during the Bush II era. I could give dozens of examples, but a particularly glaring one, cited by other reviewers, is the scene where Pollard tells Seabiscuit's owner and trainer that he is blind in one eye. Ross wants to show that his hero, Seabiscuit's owner, is not the kind of guy who would give up on someone just because he's down on is luck. Well-intentioned it may be, but as Hillenbrand's book makes clear, in real life Pollard would have been banned from racing for life had anyone known about his partial blindness. Hillenbrand's fans will also miss her breakneck pacing.    Even so, I adore this movie and have watched it with my kids at least 20 times. As one review I read at the time of its release said (in effect), \\"This is not that great of a movie, so how come I burst into tears every 15 minutes?\\" The thing is, the story is really that moving, that often. I can't say how much I appreciate the times when Ross could have gone for the jugular of audience manipulation with close-ups of tears, emotional speeches and 1001 violins, but he doesn't. Instead he pulls the camera back, quiets the cheering crowd, turns the music down, and lets the audience find and feel for itself the grief, or the horror, or the romance, or the triumph over adversity. \\"Seabiscuit\\" is also blessed with a high number of genuinely funny scenes. Most \\"feel-good\\" movies make me feel like a victim of cheap film school trickery, but I find watching \\"Seabiscuit\\" to be an honestly inspiring experience.     SPOILER AHEAD    I'm also happy to report that, unlike in the book (which of course had to go there since it was a biography of a horse foaled in 1933), in the movie, Seabiscuit does not die in the end. I read a lot of books and see a lot of movies about horses. The horse almost always dies in the end, and  I hate it when that happens. It does not matter how often I've read the same book or seen the same movie; even though I know it's coming and try to steel myself, I bawl my eyes out every time the horse dies--it's embarrassing.     If you like the movie and have not read the book, read the book. Even though it will break your heart many times, the book is one of the best I've ever read.    NEGATIVE COMMENT ABOUT THE RACING INDUSTRY AHEAD    Horse racing is a thrilling sport, as you'll learn from the book and the movie. It's also a sport with a a dark underbelly that neither the book nor the movie explores. I understand, at least at a superficial level, the difference between the humane approach and the animal rights approach. I personally fall into the former category. Without getting into that philosophical debate, the troubles with racing from the humane perspective are numerous, but the main ones are these. The thoroughbred industry breeds too many horses. A horse is not a dog or a cat: he needs a lot of space,  a lot of food, costly vet care and normally lives 25 years or more. His racing career is typically over no later than age 6. What do we do with all the racehorses who never win money for their owners or who stop winning money for their owners? Ship them out of the US to slaughter mostly, a tragic fate for an intelligent, sensitive, beautiful animal without which human civilization would not exist as we know it. Second, while some horses bred for racing can be retrained for other careers in the show ring or lesson barn, the way horses are bred, trained and raced contributes to breakdowns that frequently render them unsuitable for other careers. Only a limited number of people can afford to keep horses as pets. Most owners need their horses to generate some income  by giving riding lessons or having paying clients compete with them in the show ring. In the quest for new speed records, thoroughbreds are bred more for speed than soundness. They are broken to ride and raced under saddle too young, before their bones and muscles are fully developed, leaving them prone to unnecessary injuries. A farrier (who is no bleeding heart by the way; his truck is covered with \\"Bush-Cheney\\" and \\"Drill Here Drill Now\\" stickers) told me that he refuses to work in the racing industry because racehorses' hooves are intentionally and unnaturally trimmed with long toes and short heels. This makes them run faster but forces them to carry their weight in an imbalanced way, again leading to unnecessary injuries. Some owners are conscientious enough and well-off enough to make lifetime commitments to their racehorses, but they're still racing them too young. I believe it's possible to reform the sport and keep it exciting, but the way the racing business is currently carried out, I refuse to support it financially. Instead Isupport horse rescues to the extent I am able.  According to Hillenbrand, gambling is what keeps the racing industry financially healthy. If anyone reads this review I would encourage you to do your own research before you go to the track or place a bet.

B001YIJOI6|4.0|Chanson Francaise is certainly an interesting genre - for every great album thats released, there are an infinite number that are plodding, dreary, and downright un-musical, despite their best intentions. Throw in the fact that much French music is now made outside France, most notably in Canada, and you've got an eclectic selection of albums to choose from - some better than others.<br /><br />French-Canadian musicians in particular are an enthralling set - only because they've given us unlikely stars in the form of Arianne Moffatt, and Pierre LaPointe, both of whom are still rather underrated. But perhaps their finest achievement has been Jerome Miniere, whose 2010 record ranked as one of the greatest albums released that year, in any language or musical genre.<br /><br />Which brings us to Coeur de Pirate, a three-word pseudonymn for young Beatrice Martin, a singer from Quebec who was just 20 at the time of the recording of this album, and who over the span of two short years has gone on to become one of France's twee-music darlings, and rightly so. Much of this appeal has to do with her inspired collaboration with Julien Dore, himself a major media draw, and their resulting duet would go on to set the French singles chart on fire, resulting in widespread exposure for this, her debut album.<br /><br />Beatrice doesn't necessarily possess a great singing voice. Like Vanessa Paradis before her, she has a very sweet, almost saccharine tonality to her voice and she is almost incapable of rising above the register she is singing in. Many critics have criticized this sort of singing as merely 'talking over songs', but I would disagree. She does indeed sing, but tends to play it safe but not attempting notes she cannot score on. Wisely, all of the songs here are easy in that respect, and she floats along on them most winningly. However, she does possess that rare quality of impeccable enunciation - every word and syllable is pronounced beautifully and audibly, and the instrumentation doesn't threaten at any stage to drown her out. Needless to say, this is a virtue that has been lost in many recent french album releases, and its a treat to be able to slip a disc in and understand every word the singer sings, more so when the music is this good.<br /><br />Of course, with every quality production, there are bound to be low points. The general consensus on this album in France is that many of the songs sounded the same. While it is difficult to argue with the rationale behind that sentiment, a couple of plays of the album would render such a claim meaningless. There are minute touches here and there that make every track a standout. Consider the playful handclaps on the backing track of \\"Berceuse\\", a highlight that make an interesting song a really good one. On the instrumental \\"Intermission\\", Beatrice's delicate piano playing takes center-stage, in a surprising move on a wholly vocal album.<br /><br />While this is very much a spring-summer album (most notably on \\"Printemps\\"), its also one that holds up remarkably well in any surrounding. Consider this as the soundtrack to a quiet night at home with a bottle of wine, or a relaxing road trip to the country, and it wouldn't be out of place in either environment. Most of the lyrics deal with love and life, but they aren't exceptionally deep or philosophical; like the album, they are breezy and light, and perfectly suited to the songs they accompany.<br /><br />The standout of course, is Beatrice's much-publicized collaboration with Julien Dore (who in 2011 released his underrated sophomore record 'Bichon'). This is one of those rare instances where the hype is justified, as the 1960s instrumentation and vintage mood to the leads' vocals lend it a quaint likeability that immediately render it as rather unique. Also, Martin & Dore's voices are very much in sync here, each complimenting the other in the most unexpected way.<br /><br />However, my own personal favorite would have to be thestripped-down, almost acoustic-sounding 'Francis', which Martin delivers with rapid-speed wordplay and the lyrics here are absolutely gripping. Although, even if you aren't a native or taught French speaker, you will find it possible to enjoy this album just based on the music and melodies, which again is a rarity. Many non-French speakers sometimes reach out to acclaimed French albums and find that they are unable to enjoy the contents. This happens if the album is heavy on songwriting and low on melody (in France, many such albums are huge chart favorites - the works of Vincent Delerm, for example, are always commercially successful, although you would be hard-pressed to hum even one tune by him; as his strength is his complex songwriting).<br /><br />Yet, there are some albums, such as this welcome debut by Coeur de Pirate, that are accessible to all. Another one in a similar vein is \\"Je Suis au Paradis\\" by Thomas Fersen from 2011, \\"Integrale\\" by L (2011), and \\"Juste Comme Ca\\" by French model turned singer Mickael Miro (2011). All of these are albums that non-native admirers of French music would enjoy, as it isn't imperative that you know the language to appreciate them.<br /><br />\\"Coeur De Pirate\\" is a short album (under 30 minutes), but like Camelia Jordana's brilliant debut, it doesn't need much time to make an impact. It also has a lot of replay value, and that something to be said for a marketplace that is often full of one-play wonders. A solid debut, and certainly an essential addition to your music collection.<br /><br />Four Stars.ich Martin delivers with rapid-speed wordplay and the lyrics here are absolutely gripping. B003ZDZ1WG|3.0|The new brand of country music, as introduced by Shania Twain back in the 1990s, has admittedly taken on way too much of a pop-air to be taken seriously as authentic country music. While Taylor Swift's music is indeed infectious, it would be unfair to classify it as country music alongside work by George Strait or even Reba McEntire. It just isn't. Whether or not this is a good thing, we now have acts such as The Band Perry, who are armed with a large number of hummable hits set firmly in the pop-country mould. Anyone would hesitate to call this pure country, but once you are past that limitation, this, their debut album, has enough tunes to ensure that it garners both critical and commercial acclaim.<br /><br />It pays to know just how young the members of The Band Perry are, and while this is very much a family affair between two brothers and a sister, it is the latter whose voice carries the entire enterprise. Kimberley Perry is a reasonably good country vocalist - I wouldn't call her a powerhouse vocalist by any means - she lacks the ability to sound convincing in the upper register, and tends to stay with flat tones and isn't especially gifted during chord progressions, but she is good at working within her comfort zone, and the songs don't attempt to overstretch her abilities either. Which is probably a good thing, as there aren't any tracks here that she flounders at. Consider the bands' breakout single \\"If I Die Young\\"; a simple tune that is effective more because of the songwriting than the vocal delivery. Some of these lyrics display a maturity beyond their age; they aren't of the confessional variety such as Swifts and aren't as menacing or typically attitudinal such as Miranda Lamberts', but they are often quiet meditations on life and love, which is why most audiences gravitate towards this band.<br /><br />On tracks such as \\"Double Heart\\", The Band Perry mixes in a few rock elements to their brand of country music, and the results are interesting. All of these songs have potential to be singles if marketed in the right manner, and its evident that the bands obvious qualities (their appearance) have been amplified to their target audience. But as a musical act, there is much they can do to improve. This would perhaps happen over time as the band grows and matures and this would possibly reflect in their songwriting, but perhaps age would also give Perry's voice a more interesting resonance, as there are some tracks here that are rather bland and plain, and not in a good way. While this makes a great `road-trip' album, it isn't one that necessarily works in all settings, which is a pity, considering newer acts like Steel Magnolia have an album out that works wonderfully in any environment. Of course, this would vary from listener to listener and the opinion is purely an individual one. Personally I find the brothers playing far more interesting than the lead singers vocals, which would benefit from a lot more depth.<br /><br />Finally, \\"The Band Perry\\" is clearly one of the more interesting new acts in country today, but in terms of musical artistry I wouldn't put them anywhere near such bands as Little Big Town or Rascal Flatts. They are pleasant, light, airy, and easy on the ears, and this is the sort of music that wont change anyones life, but works as the great soundtrack to a season, perhaps for a week or a month. If that's the sort of thing that interests you, this might be an album you could enjoy.<br /><br />Three and a half stars.ave potential to be singles if marketed in the right manner, and its evident that the bands obvious qualities (their appearance) have been amplified to their target audience. 0515149489|4.0|Some call her a victim. Eight years ago she was the lone woman to escape a serial killer's deadly web. Some call her a hero. Seven years ago her fiance and his K-9 police partner were gunned down by the killer in revenge for her escape, but it was her testimony that put the murderous sociopath behind bars. Fiona Bristow considers herself a survivor. She endured. She lived. She fought back, got away, and stepped up, then pulled herself back from the shaky precipice of grief and trauma and moved on, building a decent life for herself on Orcas Island in Washington state.<br /><br />Now Fiona is a respected dog trainer. She deals with multiple levels of group training as well as one-on-one training for dogs with behavioral issues, but she specializes in training search and rescue dogs and their handlers, and heads an S and R team that works with the local and state police on an as-needed basis. When her neighbor, the terse and blunt woodworking artist Simon Doyle pulls into her driveway with a short temper and a bundle of incorrigible puppy named Jaws, Fiona gets both a new client and an opportunity to explore an interesting amount of sexual chemistry. Her life is full with a job she loves, friends and relatives she cares about and enjoys spending time with, and dogs that are her passion and pride.<br /><br />It was an idyllic life bursting with potential until the morning one of her friends on the police force turned up at her house and told her that two women were dead and a new monster had risen to pick up where the previous one had left off, a copycat killer who seemed to be heading closer. Fiona's life was once again in the crosshairs of a killer.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0515149489 The Search]] is an appealing romantic suspense that displays much of Nora Roberts skill with an atmospheric narrative that fully submerges readers into the setting and charms them with appealing characters who are likable and sympathetic. Characterization is one of Roberts' greatest strengths, and all primary, secondary, and ancillary characters are fully formed, realistic, and three dimensional. I loved Fiona and Simon, and I adored the dogs, especially Jaws, who was great for comic relief through the first two parts of the book.<br /><br />I found the search-and-rescue aspects fascinating and well researched, and those plot threads felt authentic and added a lot of emotion to the story. The romance was thorough and satisfying, and Fiona's strength, independence, and obsessive orderliness fit well with Simon's near antisocial tendencies, his artistic temperament, and his inherent sloppiness. There was a great blend of sexual tension and emotional comfort in their relationship as it evolved, strengthened, and became significant, then imperative. Few authors are as talented as Roberts with the ability to slowly age and develop characters and bring them together stronger and better for the pairing.<br /><br />The serial killer and copycat threads were the weakest points of the story for me, and felt unoriginal and tired. The copycat in particular never struck me as anything but a pathetic poser, even as his violence escalated, and he never seemed composed and intent enough to be a realistic threat. Perhaps if his crimes had been blended in a little better than they were, or had more presence in the narrative, but the scenes that featured him seemed sparse and lacked impact.<br /><br />I'm also still confused about the the trend I've noticed in the dialogue Roberts has been writing over the last couple of years. I still haven't been able to put my finger on what it is, exactly, but it almost seems like dialogue shorthand, with a shortened sentence structure and a lack of dialogue description that would normally add tone to the characters' voices. As a result, characters often seem to be speaking in a rapid fire back-and-forth that seems devoid of emotion. It's a bit startling when compared to the fluid, descriptive, colorful narrative that so adroitly captures the setting, painting it in vibrant visual detail.<br /><br />Take those issues out of the equation, though, and [[ASIN:0515149489 The Search]] was one of the better Roberts' romances I've read lately. Fiona and Simon were strong, memorable characters who truly seemed made for each other, and I loved seeing Fiona work with the dogs. I also appreciated the lighter, humorous moments liberally spread throughout the book. There were several instances where I found myself chuckling out loud, and far more funny moments than I've ever seen in her other books. It was a very pleasant surprise, and it added another dimension to this thoroughly entertaining romance.<br /><br />~*~*~*~<br />Reviewed for One Good Book Deserves Another.etail.
