B002V091IO|5.0|Emma Morley and Dexter Mayhew have been attending the same university for four years . . . but they only properly meet on the day of graduation.<br /><br />Emma Morley is an idealistic English and History major who truly believes she can change the world (or at least her little corner of it) with hard work and passion. She has had a crush on the impossibly handsome Dexter for four years now, and is both horribly ashamed of her infatuation with someone so self-obsessed, but also terribly nervous that she will never see him after their one night together. . .<br /><br />Dexter is an anthropology student (sort of) who has breezed through university with more drunken memories than grade-point-averages. He had never really taken much notice of Emma Morley, with her thick-framed glasses and badly cut hair. But something about tonight (their last night before the impending doom of adulthood) has seen Dexter enamoured of Emma's close-lipped smile and owlish eyes. But Dexter knows how their little flirtation will end - with him walking out the door and never thinking of her again. . .<br /><br />Little do Emma and Dex (Dex and Em) know that the day they meet - July 15th, 1988 (St. Swithin's Day) - will be the beginning of a beautiful friendship that spans 20 glorious years.<br /><br />`One Day' is the best-selling 2009 novel from British writer David Nicholls.<br /><br />I have had this novel sitting on my shelf since 2009. I bought it because I loved Nicholls's `Starter for Ten' and had it on good authority that `One Day' was another incredible read. But it wasn't until I saw the trailer for the upcoming film adaptation that I was intrigued enough to finally crack the book open . . . and I'm so terribly glad that I did.<br /><br />`One Day' begins in 1988, when Emma Morley and Dexter Mayhew attempt to salvage the tatters of an attempted one-night-stand by agreeing to be civil and even, absurd as it sounds, friends. Neither of them have much faith in the other to carry through with the offer . . . but then we jump ahead to the same day one year later when Dexter is travelling (to `broaden the mind') and Emma is stuck in another dead-end job and outpouring her disappointments to her dear friend, Dex, in letter form.<br /><br />And so the novel continues. Each chapter is another year in the life of Dexter and Emma, a snapshot of the day they first met, July 15th, every year for twenty years. We read how their lives are intersecting, drifting or overlapping as they navigate an oddball friendship that amazingly stands the test of time. It's a friendship of highs and lows. Bitter rivalries and broken hearts. Infidelities  and one-night-stands.<br /><br />We meet Em and Dex in their idealistic early twenties, suffer through their mid-thirties, through to the foreboding middle-ages . . . There are years when they are each other's soul-confidante; acting as touch-stones when they suffer through the death of a parent, and post-college down-trodden idealism. Then there are the years when they slowly drift apart - Dexter's career `in the media' sees him enjoying the success and excess of fame, while Emma feels like a second-fiddle friend with a ho-hum teaching career. But they always find a way back to each other, eventually.<br /><br />Their relationship is that of opposites-almost-attracting. Emma cannot deny that she had a crush on Dexter for four long years at college. But when they become friends Emma accepts that Dexter is firmly set in his lad-about-town, caddish ways and will never see her as anything other than a friend. Likewise, Dexter is inexplicably drawn to Emma - her wry wit and earnest disposition that is so different from the usual chits he attracts. Emma and Dexter love one another - they are never better than when they are in each other's presence. They become funnier, more confident, more honest and simply better. But their romantic relationship is one of near-misses and almost's. Love letters that never get sent. Phone callsthat go unanswered. One of them enters into a relationship just as the other accepts singledom . . . all of this spanning 20 long years, until the stars finally align and Em and Dex (Dex and Em) accept what everyone else has always known about their relationship.<br /><br />I love David Nicholls. He writes a beautifully complex mash-up that's not so sugary-sweet that it's chick-lit, but is never more funny than earnest like lad-lit. He really does write cross-gender books that have enough emotional complexity and romance for female readers and wry observations by larrikin characters to appeal to the male demographic.<br /><br />Dexter Mayhew was brilliant. He's a layered character, constantly changing throughout the novel. He is initially a little bit awful - at one point deciding he wants to take up photography because he likes the idea of picking up chicks with the line \\"I'm a professional photographer\\". In the beginning he's as deep as a puddle and egotistical to boot. It's only in his scenes opposite Emma that readers are able to see his appeal. Emma makes him better - she pushes him to be honest and self-deprecating, she doesn't give him an inch and calls him out on his glib insincerity. Dexter is at his funniest when he's trying to keep up with Emma's whip-quick wit. But he's also frustrating - for as much as he is improved by Emma's proximity, and as much as readers can read his betterment around her, he insists on one-night-stands with vapid misses and stone-cold beauties. It really does take Dexter 20 years to wake up to himself and realize what has always been right in front of him.<br /><br />Emma and Dexter's relationship was brilliant to read because it's so bloody true. It's a relationship based on what's not said - all those words and truths that get lodged in your throat and stuck because you're embarrassed or it's not the right time or you might risk the friendship. It's a romance based on `what if?' and near-misses. Dexter and Emma are just two people - you may even have a Dex and Em in your friendship group. Two people who are so obviously meant to be together if they would just get their act together first!<br /><br />What really impressed me in this book was how David Nicholls avoided the `big scenes' but kept the novel interesting and the plot chugging. Each chapter is a snapshot of July 15th of that year. We only read what happens on that day - whether it be a disastrous job interview, a friend's wedding, or a trip to Cornwall. Nicholls constantly references big events that are upcoming or have just been - like the death of a parent, or an impending marriage. But Nicholls never writes the actual scene - we never see the moment in the hospital room when the mother dies. Instead we read about the fall-out and repercussions of that event. It's an interesting and unconventional way to write a book - sticking to the `one day a year' format means that (realistically) all the big moments and dramas won't all conspire to fall on that one day in July. But Nicholls hasn't let that dampen the story or lessen the drama. This insistence on writing snapshots and aftermath means that the novel really does feel like a journey, and a relatable one at that.<br /><br />`One Day' is a fantastically bittersweet story of a relationship that weathers the test of time. Emma and Dexter are two people who fell into their friendship, but managed to cling to it like a life-raft through 20 years of milestones and pitfalls, failures and triumphs . . . always with the knowledge that they were each other's constant, the one person they could depend on in the whole world. I laughed (okay, snorted too) and wept bitterly throughout the novel. David Nicholls is an epic author of ordinarily extraordinary romance and `One Day' is a must-read novel for young and old, men and women.s the other accepts singledom . . . all of this spanning 20 long years, until the stars finally align and Em and Dex (Dex and Em) accept what everyone else has always known about their relationship. B001CUXZT0|3.0|After the disco Disney, made-for-The-Muppets production over-reach of `[[ASIN:B001CBW11W Alice Cooper Goes to Hell]]', the follow-up to 1975's seminal `Welcome to My Nightmare', `Lace and Whiskey' was a welcome improvement in tone from Alice Cooper (and, I imagine, somewhat of a relief for fans).<br /><br />This time, producer Bob Ezrin brings a darker atmosphere and an edgier rock sound than he did on `Goes to Hell', but as a whole it is still on the soft side compared to `Nightmare' and the Alice Cooper Band albums. Though there is no horror or shock in these songs (and bluntly, a few are lyrically limp), there is an undercurrent of sex and wretchedness to it which becomes apparent after a few listens. This infuses the whole thing with an unexpected power that will keep bringing you back.<br /><br />The steamy rock riffs of `It's Hot Tonight' start the album off with a sense of restlessness that seems to stay with it throughout, despite the genre-hopping styles of the other tracks. As an opener, it definitely recalls the Alice Cooper Band of old. Possibly the most addictive track in this set follows with the castanet-infused title track rocker paralleling sex with alcoholism (Be as soft as you can, Put a drink in my hand, I'm as scared as I ever could be). The pace picks up a notch with the piano-driven `Road Rats', a song about roadies. Lyrically bereft it may be (We work this band cause we make it rock, But we're the guys that make it roll), but in its self justification that the star would be nothing without the roadie, it supports the developing theme of despair. The fifties-styled boogie of `Damned if You Do' follows, lyrically clunky as it is, continues the pace and theme - this time in the guise of a man at the mercy of his libido. The show-stopping sadness of the signature ballad, 'You and Me' lends gravitas before the vaudeville wink of the epic `King of the Silver Screen' injects some black humour in its construction site tale of gender dysphoria. A pointless and uninteresting cover of Jerry Lee Lewis's `Ubangi Stomp' interrupts the flow before the irresistible dark disco repetitiveness of `(No More) Love at Your Convenience' and the plaintive, heavily orchestrated `I Never Wrote Those Songs' resume the theme prior to the peculiar pomp of the album closer, `My God'.<br /><br />Though the set is a bit uneven, it is lifted by a superb vocal performance from Alice Cooper that is largely devoid of the theatricality that had hitherto characterized his singing. It may be the reason the album seems to have been so badly received. It's true that it is not in the league of `Welcome to My Nightmare' or `Killer', but it has some soaring high points, it's interesting, a bit understated and certainly worth a listen. 0071752854|5.0|As a huge fan of the author's work , and the articles on his website, I was excited last week to finally get my hands on his new effort: Pitch Anything. In this book Klaff's  basic thesis is that we pitch our deals and ideas from one part of our brain (what he calls \\"the supersmart neocortex\\") yet these are received in the other person's crocodile brain. Instant mis-match. And problems ensue. Klaff uses many examples (all are interesting and ones you haven't heard of before) to demonstrate how to solve this mis-match. He shows you these methods in action and in high-stakes situations, Such as when he was thrust into a deal worth $1 Billion. I won't reveal the outcome... it's worth the read if only to get the insider's POV on such a massive deal. This has always been Klaff's talent: taking real world deals, the big and high stakes deals, and bringing them into the public's view through great journalism and storytelling.     Klaff is also careful to examine the flipside of this phenomenon: the times when the neocortex and the crocodile brain miscommunicate, unchecked, leading him  to disastrous results. For instance, he presents examples where the mind works based on biases that don't necessarily enter the realm of conscious thought, and situations where he didn't win the deal, didn't pitch the great pitch and wasn't the most popular guy in the room. Few authors are willing to self deprecate in this way.     It's a great topic and Klaff sets it up with some fast paced and wish-i-had-been-there examples, but a word of caution. First, the book is very anecdotal. You should read the sample chapter in the Amazon Look Inside feature to see if this writing style is for you. Anyone who has ever had a 200-level psych class knows that Klaff is at the same time explaining these methods TO you, and using them ON you. Given this, I found that I read some chapters more than once, especially chapter 3. First time for the fun narrative. Second time to learn the method. Third time, to see how the method was being used on me - the reader. For instance, Klaff describes a subtle technique called Prizing. This, in essence, is a way to make the customer earn your attention (instead of you constantly trying to win his) or make an investor defend why he should be allowed to invest.  But re-read that chapter. Klaff is \\"Prizing\\" the whole way through. Well played sir.    One of Klaff's strengths is in recognizing interesting cognitive events that are recurring in the business world, and then bringing them into conscious awareness, describing them so we actually realize when these things are happening in our own business and personal dealings. I think he's partly achieved that in this book, but it doesn't come together until you fully get into chapter two, which describes frames, framing and frame control. This is important stuff, This is not a book of sales scripts, and this subject is not for dummies.          The writing is enjoyable - I read the most of it in a single plane flight. Some of the insights provide building blocks for understanding how certain professionals  are able to present themselves so much better than others. They are essentially doing  something that doesn't register at the conscious-level but they are using a real method none the less - called Hot Cognition in Klaff's lexicon. He's a good documenter of new curiosities of cognitive skills everyone needs in business (sort of like a Michael Porter is to Business Strategy) and the storytelling is good enough for a full morning's entertainment.

006145205X|5.0|Pick your favorite scenario for what you think 2100 will look like:    A) Massive natural disasters will have annihilated most major cities. Pollution and toxic waste will have made much of the planet unlivable. Those humans who survived the Earth's massive calamities will be living in small, sustainable communities, armed to protect themselves against outlaws.    B) We'll be living miserable, overpopulated, filthy lives under global governmental tyranny. We'll have to steal or eat squirrels to supplement our meager government rations. We'll constantly be under surveillance, even in our private lives. We won't be able to do anything with government permission. Global elites will have built cities apart from the world's commoners.     C) The world will be more prosperous than ever before. We'll have avoided massive global warming by uncovering new, sustainable energy sources. Most of us will live in megacities, but will still be able to visit forests, lakes, and other natural resources. We'll be well-fed, clothed, and safe.     D) Zombies!    In this book, Matt Ridley proves that we're headed not for calamity or misery. Instead, we're going to see something like option C. We'll probably have more food, less disease, more income, a longer lifespan, and better lives in general.     What makes Ridley think that things are getting better? 100,000 years of human history, which he covers in his book. He uses patterns from that span of human existence to illustrate that our capacity to change and adapt will bring us successfully through whatever problems we face. These problems even include collective boogeymen like global warming, economic crashes, and deadly diseases.     Ridley's Reality    Throughout the course of his 359-page book, Ridley tracks human evolution, from cavemen to the modern Homo Computus, to prove that the world has steadily improved over time for humans. There are two main reasons for this.    1) Trade is good.   Trade, which Ridley defines as the \\"market process of exchange and specialization,\\" is ancient. It's inherently fair, in that it creates changes that benefit everyone.     Over the years, an increase in trade has led to an increase in innovation, improving the quality of life for everyone. Free trade inevitably creates mutual prosperity (protectionism does the opposite).    2) More specialization and exchange lead to prosperity.   Prosperity is \\"the increase in the amount of goods or services you can earn with the same amount of work.\\" Increasing specialization and more exchange are the root cause of innovation.     When exchange, invention, and specialization proliferate, a society \\"creates\\" time. As a result of this proliferation, modern people have specialized occupations, but consume hundreds of diverse things. This is the definition of a higher standard of living.    Much of <em>The Rational Optimist</em> comes back to these two main points. Ridley makes a slew of other, smaller points in the process, always using research and historical examples as proof. The following caught me eye:    * Commerce has helped make the \\"is as good a place for the average human being to live as it ever has been.\\" Capitalist success, in other words, leads to social improvements. Moreover, Ridley finds that \\"people get happier as they get richer.\\" Money does buy \\"general well-being.\\"     * Self-sufficiency leads to poverty. This is because bigger, more connected populations innovate more, while smaller, less connected populations tend to regress. Human beings depend on numbers, connections, and trade to succeed. So our nostalgia for farming and sustainability is just that--nostalgia. It doesn't represent progress.     * City dwellers are better for the ecosystem. Cities house half the world's people while using less than 3% of global land area. Country dwellers take up more space, use more energy, and impact the environment less.     * Fossil fuels like gas and coal, as well as the machines you use every day, have replaced human labor (slaves) over time. So, in a sense, they are your slaves.     * When the world started relying on fossil fuels for energy, economic growth became sustainable for the first time in history.     The Rational Optimist's 10 rich chapters include innumerable examples from diverse fields, including anthropology, history, biology, economics, and even Hollywood movies. These examples, as well as Ridley's own skill as a writer--and occasional cheekiness--make for an entertaining, if highly detailed, read.     Thoughts    The Rational Optimist has all of the elements of my favorite kind of book:    * The author uses studies, research, surveys, and solid evidence to make his claims.    * It covers business and the economy, but with emphasis on human behavior.    * It takes the long view of humanity, covering around 100,000 years, rather than elaborating on a fad or short period of time.    * It's entertaining.     Perhaps most importantly, The Rational Optimist challenged me to see things in a new way. For example, Ridley makes the case that although global warming is happening, it's completely hyped. The net harm from expected global warming will not be as big as the harm being done today by hunger, dirty water, indoor smoke, and malaria, according to Ridley. If the globe indeed warms as much as we expect it will, \\"it will be because more people are rich enough to afford to do something about it.\\"     Indeed, Ridley makes a number of points that would be considered biased towards economic conservatism, from an American viewpoint. He supports free trade and thinks all governments (he calls them monopolies) eventually mess up innovation. Yet innovation and open trade are necessary for a country to prosper, so it's odd that smart people put too much faith in government for no good reason. He is pro-fossil fuel, pro-city, pro-genetically modified foods, and optimistic about humanity in general.     Yet instead of using rhetoric, he employs evidence and centuries ofhuman history to make his points. This is precisely what makes The Rational Optimist so valuable.     Recommended for Some    I welcome new, intelligent perspectives on human life, so I happily devoured The Rational Optimist. Yet I could see Ridley, who isn't shy about bashing leading thinkers (and especially environmentalist Paul Ehrlich) irking other readers. If you happen to be an extreme environmentalist/liberal/fan of organics, this book might tee you off, depending on how staunch your views are. Ditto if you want something quick and to-the-point, rather than long and detailed.     If you like seeing things debunked, however, pay attention to The Rational Optimist--you might like it. Same if you like reading about human history for hours, if you like having your viewpoints challenged, and if you enjoy laughing out loud on occasion while reading. This has been one of the best economics-related books I've read in the past couple of years, so I do recommend it overall.     (Book review by Drea Knufken)

0071752854|5.0|Oren Klaff has a dazzling ability to clearly describe some new methods for presenting ideas .<br /><br />As he displays in this entertaining and illuminating look at how we pitch deals --ideas, projects or even ourselves --he can parse for general readers the intricacies of fascinating but little-known methods like Prizing (eg. why should you never ask for the deal). Klaff's conclusion, after studying how people make decisions in a wide range of situations from the boardroom to the common coffee shop meeting, is that we can make better pitches  by never being needy, by elevating our status with something called local star power--and that less information (as long as it's the right information) is better than more.<br /><br />Perhaps the most stunning example he gives of this counterintuitive truth is the time he faced off with a powerful hedge fund manager, in which the rich and powerful banker tried to ignore Klaff during a deal, yet the author was able to reframe the situation in a surprising way. This incident, by the way, is worth the price of admission for the book.<br /><br />But if one sets aside Klaff's dazzle, some questions emerge. If pitching is an algorithm, or formula, such as Klaff's method, in which only a few steps are needed to get a deal into the waiting arms of an investor or buyer, is that really increasing your ability--or is it taking it out of your hands altogether and handing it over to the algorithm? Still, each case study in the book is satisfying, and Klaff imparts his own evident pleasure in finding new methods that have not been used before and seeking the best possible way to pitch every deal.

B000VI6T6K|5.0|If you're on this page, then you're probably already a Hendrix fan, so you have a pretty good idea of the insanely chaotic and beautiful energy he can unleash when he plays his instrument.The thing to know about this album, however, is that it's the best live album in the Hendrix catalogue, and possibly the best live album ever.    In my opinion, the original lineup of the Jimi Hendrix Experience (Hendrix on guitar, Mitch Mitchel on drums, and Noel Redding on bass guitar) is by far superior to his later Band of Gypsys. While Billy Cox of the Gypsys mainly concerns himself with repititive grooves, Mitchel's drumming is more energetic and propulsive, and altogether much more \\"rock\\", like his fellow Brits Ginger Baker of Cream and Keith Moon of The Who. Redding and Miles are different in the same way.    Most of the songs on this album are fast and extremely loud. The opener, \\"Killing Floor\\" is a thrashy, proto-metal rocker while the finale, \\"Wild Thing\\", with its droning feedback ending, is a precursor to noise rock. I'm not sure, but I think that's the part of the show where he lights his guitar on fire.    I seem to hear Jimi Hendrix's later live stuff praised more for being more \\"natural\\" and Blues-oriented. One review I read a while ago even brought his race into the issue, asserting that earlier noise-heavy shows were degrading to his fellow black musicians. I had never read something more stupid written so eloguently in a music review. It's a classic case of critics worshipping the Blues and crapping on Heavy Metal.    Anyway, as a Metal fan I prefer \\"Live at Monterey\\".

The Mob Rules was not so kind and experience, and it eventually resulted in Dio being asked to leave the Black Sabbath. For me, it's an effort as important in the Black Sabbath canon as the albums \\"Black Sabbath\\" and \\"Sabbath Bloody Sabbath\\"; as musical creations, these albums elevated Black Sabbath to heights that were greater than their contemporaries. Mob Rules is without a doubt the heaviest Black Sabbath album in years. Indeed, it's heavier than a lot of other metal acts of that era. Just as ACDC and Van Halen were beginning to enjoy their commercial success, and their interest in maintaining it, Black Sabbath took the other route and made an album that was meant to be played loud and hard. The production is thick and relentlessly overpowering. Kudos to Geezer Butler's earthquake inducing bass riffs. Tonni Iomi's guitar has never sounded more dense and threatening. But don't forget Dio's vocals. There are Herculean in their strength and intensity. This album is all about power, and there is no band more powerful than Black Sabbath.
0618918248|5.0|With The God Delusion, Dawkins writes a clear, interesting, and thorough indictment of religion. Contrary to those who state that he words it too harshly, I found his writing to be clear, comprehensible, and often laced with gentle humor at just the right moment. While his argument is logical, he deftly keeps it from being dry or boring.    One of the very points that he brings up in the book is the concept that religion should for some unknown reason be immune from criticism. As one might imagine, Dawkins vigorously dissents from this viewpoint and asserts that religion should be just as subject to criticism as any other idea or concept.    And criticize he does. I would advise both theist and atheist readers to read this title. It is not a bad thing to ask \\"Is what I think defensible? Is it correct? How should I reexamine it?\\" Though I had already long since drifted away from the idea of any gods existing by the time I read this, it does make clear what is at stake, and how religion can be actively harmful on both an individual and societal level. It also examines the assertions that religion can be beneficial.    If you don't want to think, by all means avoid this book. If you would like what you think to be challenged (and Dawkins has challenges for atheist and theist alike), I would strongly advise that you read it. The one thing I took away from the book is something I hope we can all agree on: Critically examining our thoughts and beliefs, and changing them if they are indefensible, is necessary for us to become better people. We should never accept \\"Because I said so\\".

B002ZG993Q|4.0|Yes, it features perhaps the biggest Hollywood icon of our generation in the lead, but The American is very much a subtle, european exploration of the much fabled hitman genre.  George Clooney is Jack, an expert hitman on the run, laying as low as he can in the country hillsides of Italy.  There, his past, present, and future all collide in an exploration of how a man comes to terms with his soul, glimpsing a future he had never before thought to examine.<br /><br />While many have called this film flat out boring, the truth is The American is filmed, set, and narrated much in the tradition of mid 1900's european films.  What does that mean?  Symbolism over sensational violence.  Very few characters, singular plot lines driven to help the main character find his transformative way.  Indeed, if you're looking for 10 cool guys/girls to weave a storyline in 10 different directions, this is not your film.<br /><br />Simply put, this film plays out much like a novel.  Hitman novels, in fact, for years have been a subgenre of amazing acclaim yet little mainstream fanfare.  The American will remind you more of classic assassin books like 'Stiletto', Thomas Perry's 'The Butcher's Boy', or even Lawrence Block's \\"Hitman\\" series than recent films like 'The Machinist\\" (remake)or \\"Lucky number slevin\\"<br /><br />That being said, I'm not sure i've ever seen a film carry a more novelistic feel, and it serves as an amazing canvas for an actor of Clooney's calibre to articulate a character filled with inner angst, mentally and cerebrally powerful, yet emotionally vulnerable.  A subtle yet outstanding performance that in the Taoist sense gives us \\"action through nonaction\\"

But for all his courage in overcoming his handicap, he keeps the story from moving forward until the last thirty minutes when the story's stature becomes more important because of the impending threat from Hitler and WWII.<br /><br />However, it must be said that Colin Firth plays King George VI with gusto, matched only by Geoffrey Rush as Mr. Logue, his therapist. The two of them create sparks in many a confrontational moment of truth, and each has a firm grip on their roles. Equally proficient, but less noticeable and given a smaller amount of screen time, is Helena Bonham Carter as the gracious and adoring woman who stands by her stubborn man and presides over a household of two children, one of whom is a future Queen of England. In a lesser but still rich role, Guy Pearce is excellent as Edward VIII.<br /><br />But unfortunately, because of the film's preoccupation with King George's speech impediment, the film moves forward at a snail's pace and only becomes more urgent during the final half hour when the world awaits the king's reaction to the news that Great Britain has failed to prevent war with Germany.<br /><br />All the technical merits make the film outstanding in various departments, particularly the cinematography, costumes, settings and music. I purchased a Analog-USB adapter so I don't have to deal with pulling each connector out individually.    Audio:       The audio sounds tinny and distant, like a cheap pair of Apple headphones. I doubt adjusting the EQ will change anything. If you are buying these to listen to music, look elsewhere. The microphone is clear and sensitive (my housemate owns another pair and we chat during gaming).    Comfort:       The headsets cling firmly to your head without falling off, even when I try \\"ye olde shake-your-head\\" test. The velvety padding is very thick and comfortable, much better than the fake leather and plastic that most other lower-end headsets use. Velvet is a great choice because it won't stick to your skin, or tear and become an annoyance. The padding on the portion that goes over your head is 3/4 inch thick, and has a memory foam-like consistency. I have a big head, but I don't feel that it's a tight fit. I'm sure that it will break in over time and either become more comfortable or be too loose.     Overall:        You get what you pay for. For $24 dollars with 2-day shipping, nothing can beat it. Great for gaming, but poor for listening to music. It serves its purpose. I hope this review will help you make your decision.

0312600844|4.0|This is an interesting book that slowly and stealthily gathers momentum and power. Like building a Lego castle, the precise, almost clinical observations of individual players connect to the larger theme of the contemporary American experience; the semblance of freedom yanking the chains of inner inflexibility, emotional insecurity and confusion. The author has an outstanding understanding of human psychology and social dynamics that can only reflect a remarkable inner clarity and self-understanding. Reading between the lines, i believe i can detect  an elusiveness, aloofness, irony and desire to be cool as well as a curious detachment and lack of passion.<br /><br />There is no good or evil as far as Franzen is concerned, just more or less deluded folks trying to make it through another day. We may want to love each other, to be independent, free and happy but we don;t know how. Even Republican venality, ignorance and short-termist greed hellbent on destroying the environment through mountain-top mining are mocked between the lines as lack of consciousness and social brainwashing. There are insightful vignettes describing Kristol-like NYC/DC neocons, T. Boone Pickens-like oilmen; aging rockers and the sad, insular & desperate world that represents a large part of the  alternative NYC art scene.<br /><br />The trajectories of lives in Freedom are tinged with sadness and regret, much of the book is permeated by quiet desperation which at times appears close to nihilistic. Even (or especially) when it is funny. Franzen's universe appears to be driven by human frailty, decency, (mostly) unexamined emotions and coincidence which merge into the large stream of American Life. There is no magic. Little inspiration. A curious sort of emptiness and a lack, perhaps, of soul. Walter's love of birds was a tiny little spark of light in the darkness composed of human frailty. Yet isn't it frailty, when acknowledged, that is required for authenticity? The  pain of living includes freedom for making  mistakes, correcting them or choosing to die with things left unsaid. I felt that people like Walter (a F. Doppelgaenger perhaps?) and Patty are preordained to suffer because they somehow (unconsciously? reflexively? helplessly?) see the bigger picture of what matters. Life catches them on a hook, like fish gasping in the air.<br /><br />Almost in spite of itself, an innate will to live and will to love surfaces out of nowhere at the end when the book reveals itself as a love story.<br /><br />I found the book dense & slow at the start, but it got to me after i slogged through ~half. I am glad i read it.mistakes, correcting them or choosing to die with things left unsaid. I felt that people like Walter (a F. B001VZY4M8|4.0|A recent hearing of Neil Young's 2005 CD PRAIRIE WIND got me to thinking that this always interesting singer songwriter had indeed matured in significant ways.  While I'm not going to argue that his early recordings remain among his most definitive--and certainly his most beloved--I can't help concluding that his more recent work is more polished, more direct and better developed than much of the early stuff.    In his earliest days, Neil Young could be downright cryptic.  I remember that, as early as BUFFALO SPRINGFIELD AGAIN, I found myself (budding teenage critic that I was) carping that a song like \\"Broken Arrow\\" only made sense if you either knew in advance just what, in indigenous American symbolism, a \\"broken arrow\\" stood for (namely, giving up the fight) OR if, like me, you happened to read all about it in an interview with the songwriter.    Yes, Young could be somewhat hard to decipher back then--as were so many other aspiring singer-songwriters held under the sway of Bobby Dylan.  And no, I'm not complaining.  Neil Young did better than most in the obcurantism department.  You kind of always knew what he was talking about, even when you didn't quite.  Did he really shoot \\"his baby, down by the river\\" or not?  Well, something bad happened, either way, but it might've just been metaphorical, right? Right.    It's pretty much the same with the title song of this, his second solo album (without Crazy Horse, I mean).  Story goes that when Ronstadt, Harris and Parton were recording that tune for the TRIO album, they gave its author a call to ask  him what the heck it all meant.  Neil's response: \\"How should I know?  I just wrote it.  Probably depends on what I was on at the time.\\"  Personally, I've always felt it had something to do with the \\"Wooden Ships\\"/BLOWS AGAINST THE EMPIRE kind of millenniumism that was so au courant in the late 60s/early 70s.  Escaping in a silver spaceship to a \\"new home in the sun\\" sounded pretty good, especially given that \\"Mother Nature\\" was \\"on the run in the 1970s\\"--and has been doing all that well in the 40 years since either.    But significantly, for Young, it WAS a dream--one of several in the song--not the impending \\"reality\\" that Paul Kanter, say, seemed to be envisioning in his magnum opus mentioned above.  Neil Young remained something of a skeptic in the face of all that Age of Aquarius/Starship Enterprise bushwa, and it's to his lasting credit.  Still keeping it cryptic helped him hedge his bets at the time.  He could embrace counter culture values, while doubting their efficacy at the same time.  It worked for Dylan, of course, and for others (Grace Slick and Joni Mitchell, on the distaff side--although Joni's tranistion from self-conscious naif to worldly wise cynic was more abrupt).    Speaking of naivete, was the fact that Neil Young was, like Mitchell, Canadian and therefore at something of a remove from U.S. culture that led to something as (inadvertantly?) confrontational as \\"Southern Man.\\"   I say \\"confrontational\\" NOT \\"controversial,\\" since the song is plainly meant to be the latter.  But \\"Southern Man\\" is such a sweeping indictment of the U.S. South that you almost had to know that someone would eventually write a \\"Sweet Home Alabama\\" response song to counter it.  Actually, I may be misspeaking a bit: Young DOES suggest that \\"change [is] gonna come at last/Now your crosses are burning fast\\"--meaning, one assumes, that barbarities such cross burning will soon be a thing of the past--so he IS placing hope in emerging cultural change--precisely from Ronnie Van Zant's generation.  But diplomacy is everything in politics if not in art, so the Lynyrd Skynyrd answer piece was all but inevitable--as was the Neil Young put-down therein.    But hey, they made up apparently, what with Neil doing \\"Sweet Home\\" in his own concerts AND Ronnie Van Zant wearing Neil Young t-shirts onstage.  Alls well that ends well, I guess.  My only point is that unlike other rock and folk songwriters of the era: Neil wasn't just about \\"epater les bourgeois,\\" or \\"l'establishment.\\"  Unintentionally perhaps, he was \\"epater-ing\\" young \\"Southern men and women,\\" potential Neil Young fans all.    All water over the dam now, of course, but while I wouldn't want \\"Southern Man\\" or \\"After the Goldrush\\" unwritten,  I find the older, wiser Neil Young somewhat more interesting in  his stark, wintry way.  I wouldn't say that Neil had \\"yet to find  his voice\\" (to cite a too-oft rendered critical cliche).  His early work was very much his voice THEN.  And musically, he was penning some of the best tunes of his life.  But AFTER THE GOLDRUSH is still a \\"young man's Young.\\"  Nowadays he almost certainly identifies even more readily with the \\"Old Man\\" he was \\"a lot like\\" in 1970.  Times change:artists grow in interesting, sometimes surprising ways.    And maybe that's the point to end on. Neil Young is full of surprises, as the erratic nature of his solo career attests.  I remember when I was 18 being a little disappointed in the relative \\"prettiness\\" of AFTER THE GOLDRUSH, and later still in HARVEST.  I liked the proto-punky, jagged edges of EVERYBODY KNOWS THIS IS NOWHERE and was wary of all the  \\"mellowing out\\" occurring at the time.  Didn't seem to be in the spirit of rock'n'roll to me.  Now I find it hard to understand my reaction, at least as it pertained to the ever-unpredictable Mr. Young.      It was Neil Young, after all, who almost single-handedly brought a bit of edginess to the CSN enterprise (although Crosby seemed to be genuinely trying at times).  Similarly, on AFTER THE GOLDRUSH, Neil allowed himself to rock out a bit on the aforementioned \\"Southern Man\\" and \\"When You Dance.\\"  Looking back, I may have blamed him a bit for the fact that in 1970, every sensitive 18 year old with an acoustic guitar was trotting out their covers of \\"Tell Me Why\\" or \\"Sugar Mountain\\" at college open mics everywhere.  Hardly Young's fault after all--and they're certainly not bad tunes, butthey are just one part of the array of styles and colors he dabbled in over the course of his 40+ year career.      Seems pretty remarkable looking back on it.

I inserted a flash drive and the Soundfly Aux categorized the files in their own folders, which are easily accessed by the remote control. I found the ability to tailor the in-car soundfield to my liking with the Soundfly Aux's bass, treble, and \\"dynamic audio booster\\" circuit (which gives a very subtle increase in dynamics that seems to put more life into the MP3 formatted music). The one and a half inch screen that can be angled for best view, can be set with a contrast adjustment to allow clear viewing, and gives all the info needed to set this up for any particular taste. Everything is very easy to use.    I can see that whoever designed this little interface really took the time to design it well. If I had an MP3 player, it'll access the files on it and play them thru the head unit.     But, even after all of the little things this Soundfly Aux does so well mechanically and makes it such a great digital music interface for my particular application, the bottom line for me is that it, surprise, surprise, makes MP3 files sound so great. This is one fantastic sounding car audio digital music interface. And a perfect match up for a Mazda2.     Highly recommended!

0765350386|5.0|This is one of my absolute favorite books in the fantasy genre.  It has some incredibly compelling characters, an extremely well-constructed universe, and a plot that works extremely well.  Most importantly, though, it has by far the most interesting and well fleshed-out magical system of any book series I've ever read.  In most books, the magical system bends to accomodate the needs of the plot, but the metal-based magical system of Mistborn is almost mathematical in its precision.  The magic is inherently limited, but this allows Sanderson to do some very clever things with it, as the characters exploit these limited abilities to their fullest.  It also means that at no point is a character doing something that shouldn't be possible, much as it may look like they are at first.  It's this that really sets Mistborn apart from other fantasy series, and makes it such a worthwhile read.    The other books in the series are also well worth reading (and help flesh out the details of the universe), but I absolutely encourage you to give this one a try to start.

1151097764|3.0|A farm kid from Upstate New York, I grew up thinking of New York City as a place to be afraid of. I never went there, of course, but according to TV -- well, excluding Seinfeld -- and the movies, it was a jagged skyline towering above a huge steaming cesspool, where it was always nighttime and horrifying things happened to people. But as I got older and bored with my home county, that image began to appeal to me, and it's ultimately what brought me here.    It was that same city -- the dim grimy hole of crime and perversion, the New York of the 1980s -- that appealed to Tom Wolfe and inspired him to write 'The Bonfire of the Vanities.' Born into a genteel Virginia family, Wolfe has always been obsessed with race and class, and 'Bonfire' depicts a divided city: ethnically, politically and geographically. According to Wolfe's demographic map, in Manhattan below 96th Street is the insulated upper class of WASPs, who rarely set foot on the streets of their city. They live far above in high-rises and get ferried to and from work in taxi cabs. Also on the island are the Jews, who, after generations of struggle hold most of the positions in the city government but find power slipping from their grasp as New York demographically darkens. Then there are the masses of impoverished blacks and Puerto Ricans holding the upper half of Manhattan and nearly all of the Bronx and Brooklyn. It's clear from early in the novel that the future of the city is theirs: Riverdale is \\"a little freeport\\"; Brooklyn Heights and Park Slope \\"Hong Kongs\\"; the Bronx County Building is repeatedly called \\"Gibraltar.\\" For now they're getting chewed up by the justice system, but inevitably they'll organize and take the city. Acting as a buffer are the Queens-based lower-middle class Irish and, to a lesser degree, Italians who run the police and hold minor jobs in the city courts. Finally, there's the bohemian British expatriate community, in control of much of the city's press and motivated mainly by their contempt for Americans.    In terms of plot, Bonfire is the story of Sherman McCoy, a Wall Street bond trader from Park Avenue on the Upper East Side, born rich and getting richer. One night, while escorting his mistress from LaGuardia airport in his Mercedes-Benz, McCoy gets stuck in the wrong lane on the Grand Central Parkway and finds himself in the South Bronx. There he gets into a hit-and-run with a black high schooler that isn't entirely his fault. A good chunk of the book just consists of McCoy trembling over whether he'll get caught. But the boy's mother goes to the Reverend Bacon -- an Al Sharpton-style rabble-rouser who bends idealistic rhetoric to the purposes of intimidation and extortion -- and Bacon goes to the police.    The case lands in the lap of Lawrence Kramer, a Bronx assistant DA (one of hundreds) feeling trapped in his job and small apartment, sick of anonymity and tired of prosecuting young blacks and Puerto Ricans. However, Kramer doesn't fully realize what he has until Bacon's lawyer brings the story to Peter Fallow, an alcoholic English reporter on the verge of losing his job. When it breaks in the press, the police trace the crime back to McCoy and the car accident off Bruckner Boulevard becomes a collision of every faction in New York City. Black and Puerto Rican protestors storm Park Avenue. McCoy is dragged into a jail cell in the Bronx. The justice system revels in finally having a WASP in its teeth. Faced with a rallying block of minority voters, the Jewish politicians have no choice but to condemn the rich bond trader. McCoy hires a street-smart Irish attorney named Tommy Killian, who acquaints him with the seamier sides of the law. Ultimately, McCoy lets himself sink and embraces the violence and seediness of New York City.    If done as a drama 'Bonfire' would be painfully overwrought. But Wolfe is a satirist, and the book's outrageousness is all for the sake of comedy. The opening chapter with the mayor, the descriptions of high-class parties, of the protests, of the all-out brawl in the courtroom, of just about every scene involving Killian, are so over-the-top that the reader has to laugh. The book is also a kind of prophecy: in the '80s, an encounter like McCoy's \\"fracas in the jungle\\" probably seemed inevitable; the contradiction of the city's incredible wealth and unfathomable poverty was too great, it had to be resolved somehow. From Wolfe's perspective, as the white working class bled out of New York, the democratic process would leave the blacks and Puerto Ricans in charge, exposing the elite to the raw reality of their \\"Third World city.\\"    Of course, as I -- and countless others who've come to New York with the vision of a drippy, neon-lit metropolis -- found, that prediction proved completely wrong. In the '90s, crime was fiercely suppressed. The rich whites are richer than ever. New York has another Jewish mayor, now in his third term. Black and Puerto Rican communities are caught in a pincer between gentrification and the influx of Dominican, Mexican and Chinese immigrants. And while few neighborhoods have a strong Irish character anymore, and Italians are ceding their old strongholds in Brooklyn and Queens, the city has a growing population of white working class Eastern Europeans.    Which brings us to the question of the book's literary merits. Wolfe declared in 1989 that he'd revived the great realist tradition of the late 1800s and early 20th Century. But Bonfire is too much a portrait of a specific time and place and not enough of a novel. The book's leading men -- McCoy, Kramer, Bacon, Fallow -- are more or less the same character: vain, arrogant, sleazy, hungry for glory. Reading their interior monologues is a bit like watching a puppet show where all the dolls have the same voice. Some Internet troll will probably argue that Wolfe was trying to show the universality of certain human tendencies, but it's idiotic to think that people from such disparate backgroundswould have identical personalities. McCoy's third act conversion to a tough guy comes off as childish, and the plot is altogether too dependent on coincidence.    New York is amorphous and unpredictable, and any book whose main virtue is describing it accurately is doomed to be short-lived. 'Bonfire' is like an old Polaroid: blurring, curling at the edges, slowly turning brown and falling apart -- a reminder of the impermanence of things and of how shallow pictures really are. Like me, most people who glance at it today probably do so out of nostalgia for the dirty, gritty old city. Topical writers like Charles Dickens and John Steinbeck have endured because of their wide appeal and deep humanity. But Wolfe's writing isn't as powerful as Steinbeck's, and his characters lack the warmth and charm of Dickens'. Even though Dickens' London and Steinbeck's California have long vanished, in 100 years -- assuming anyone is still reading -- 'Great Expectations' and 'Of Mice and Men' will continue to enjoy broad audiences. But as Tom Wolfe's New York fades out of memory, 'The Bonfire of the Vanities' will likely do the same.    (...)

Half of the gods want to kill him (he made enemies by stealing Fragarach from the alpha male head honcho a long time ago and has been on the run ever since) and the other half want to use him as a pawn in their power struggles and learn his secrets of long life and protection from magic. As the story begins, his whereabouts are uncovered - but instead of running, Atticus chooses to stand and fight. So begins what is promised to be a trilogy of adventure stories pitting Atticus against deities, witches, demons, and more.    The world, where the old gods have power proportional to the strength of their followers' beliefs, and where gods can appear in human-like form and expose themselves to mortal danger, is reminiscent of the world Neil Gaiman created in American Gods and Anansi Boys. However, Hearne has forged something really special here - a page-turning romp that is as full of action and suspense as it is humor and wit. B0040UAYI4|4.0|/// First impressions:    The build quality of the EEC is not wonderful. You're paying a premium price for an emulation device, and I expected a little more for what I gave. However, it does come with a very generous 13 foot usb cable, so you'll have no trouble finding a comfortable spot to use it from.      /// Installation:    I tried three computers before I found one that would allow me to install the drivers and firmware necessary to be able to engage the EEC's key mapping functionality. I tried on a two Vista 32 Laptops, which told me when I used the automated install software that \\"it was unable to update for an unknown reason\\". Trying to manually install, and when I pointed at the drivers it just didn't see them. Had a search on the Penguinunited forums, and a few users had had this issue in the past with no resolution.     I tried on an XP 32 laptop and it took right away.     The devs claim compatibility with Vista, but I would aim to use an XP 32 PC if you can just in case.      /// Practise:    Once I got the EEC working, it was very simple to set up. The button mapping is clear and simple, and the advanced set-up for sensitivity and dead zone configuration was a lot easier than I'd thought it would be. The advanced set-up consists of tapping a designated key and scrolling the mouse wheel to secure a point with your in game cursor. It's really easy, and well explained via the provided instructions.    I set the device to work with Brink. It works very well. So well that my game's gone up about 50%. It's not quite PC accuracy, but it's a /lot/ better than attempting to be pin point with your thumbs.    There is a tricky learning curve, however, as you have to unlearn a little of what you know about mice.     If you use a mouse on a PC game, your avatar will turn at different speeds according to the speed with which you move the mouse, IE: if you move the mouse slowly, your character moves slowly; if you move the mouse quickly, your character will turn quickly. The analog sticks work to a set speed depending on where they're held, not how quickly you move them.    What this means is when you're using the EEC, if you snap your mouse for a quick 180 turn like you would for a PC title, the playstation registers only a brief tap on the analog and you will hardly move at all. If you make a long, steady sweep, the playstation will register this as the analog being held and you will move full speed.    So instead of playing with small sweeps and quick sharp movements like you might on a PC FPS, you have to make sure you hold your mouse grounded and make full gestures. It feels a little odd at first, perhaps \\"a little sticky\\" is the best way to describe it, but it'll become second nature in no time if you're an adept gamer (and I'd imagine you are if you're even considering a product like this).    Once you have the hang of it, the edge the EEC gives you is very noticeable. I'd be hesitant to use it in anything above semi-serious comp play in multiplayer as it really does feel like cheating at times.      The only other game I've tried it with so far is Mass Effect 2. It would be ideal for this game were it not for the weapon and power selection wheels. The wheels are mapped to the left anlaog stick, which can cater for more points of reference than the WASD format that the EEC uses for that control aspect. This means you can't access certain points on the command wheel.    Just beware of the control mechanics in game before you choose this for a specific title. If you're unsure, then a quick google using site:penguinunited.com as the prefix to a game title you're interested in will no doubt give you some information on whether the game's been mapped successfully for.    I would try out the other FPS I own, but I've had to give the XP laptop back to its owner. I will update my reivew in a month or so when I've had the chance to test it.       /// Overall:  - Build Quality - Average (Very generous USB lead)  - Ease of Installation - Unpredictable, but easy to follow guidelines (Xp machine recommended)  - Ease of configuration - Very simple and quick (requires access to the pc you installed on to change key maps)  - Functionality - Excellent (very noticeable increase in accuracy)    Summary - Even though it lacks in physical construction, it emulates the PC gaming experience very well indeed, and while not /as/ precise, the improvement is noticeable enough to justify the price. Highly recommended for FPS enthusiasts who struggle with analog controls.

B0000256VK|5.0|Roxy Music's Avalon is a landmark recording of the 1980s. The last studio release of the band, which came out in 1982, Avalon is just about as perfect as an album can possibly be. Through 10 tracks starting with the iconic and often covered More Than This, Bryan Ferry and company take the listener on an incredible, textured, rich musical journey. While more recent Ferry releases are all atmosphere and style, Avalon has the songs. Most of the music on Avalon was written by Bryan Ferry, while Andy MacKay and the incomparable guitarist Phil Manzanera are credited with three co-writes. At this stage, Roxy Music featured Bryan Ferry (keyboards and vocals), Phil Manzanera (guitar), Andy MacKay (saxophone), Neil Hubbard (guitar), Alan Spenner (bass), Andy Newmark (drums), Jimmy Maclen (percussion) and Fonzi Thornton (background vocals). Avalon was recorded at the Powerstation in New York by longtime Roxy Music collaborators Rhett Davies and Bob Clearmountain. Every song here is a winner: More Than This, The Space Between, Avalon, India, While My Heart Is Still Beating, Take A Chance With Me, To Turn You On, True to Life and the closer Tara. In 2003 Virgin released a remastered version of Avalon on Hybrid Super Audio CD using surround sound. Davies and Clearmountain supervised the remastering process. I haven't heard the remaster -- so I can't comment on the differences in sound between the original release -- but will be getting it soon. No album collection is complete without Roxy Music's Avalon.

B001RCD2DW|5.0|3 Main Things:<br /><br />1. They sound better than any other headphone I've had the opportunity to try.<br /><br />2. They need to be paired with an adequate amplifier for best results.<br /><br />3. They can get a little bit uncomfortable with extended use.<br /><br />It's been a few months since I've purchased these bad boys, so I figured it was about time to post my thoughts (although I still haven't hit the end of the break-in period yet).  The AKG K702s are absolutely fantastic headphones.  They offer the best sound I've ever heard in any headphone, and I've had the privilege to sample more than a few high-end sets.<br /><br />The sound is very clean, first off.  Assuming that you have paired them with an adequate source of amplification and are starting with high quality files or music source, the K702s have absolutely no detectible distortion up to extremely high volume levels (the kind that are probably damaging to your hearing).  Secondly, the sound is very discernable.  Each spectrum is nicely sectioned off and allows for full appreciation of every piece of the sound spectrum from the very very lows to the extremely high trebles.  I have found, however, that increasing the bass level on my amplifier leads to the best overall listening experience.<br /><br />They simply demand adequate amplification.  When used with a low-power audio source like an iPod, these cans just simply can't get loud enough and are pretty much wasted.  You'd be better off with a lower-end, yet less power-hungry set for that sort of setup.  If your main use is going to be mobile, I'd suggest looking somewhere else.  But, if you do have an adequate amplifier for the majority of your use, there is simply no better option at this price.<br /><br />Lastly, they do get a bit uncomfortable with extended use.  Although I put up with it for the outstanding sound quality and have gotten used to it to some degree, I still notice the pressure that the K702s put on the top of my head.  I am not sure how any of these drawbacks could be mitigated by a better design... it might just be an intractable problem with headphones of this size.<br /><br />Edit: I have mitigated the comfort issue by rubber-banding a sweatband onto the piece that rests on your head.  Still sounding great, still highly recommend.<br /><br />P.S. Purchase experience on Amazon was pain free. All I knew was that I wanted to read an Evelyn Waugh novel, and this one seemed to be most well known and well respected of the bunch.     This was quite a good read. The narrator's voice is laced with humor as well as quiet reflection, and he captivated me from the beginning. The first half of the novel, chronicling Charles Ryder's friendship with an eccentric young aristocrat, is the better half -- entertaining, original, quirky, unexpected. The second half takes the novel in a more conventional direction (though there was still plenty to keep me going).    Beyond the social commentary and homoerotic undertones which are often central to people's analyses of the novel, Brideshead Revisited is first and foremost just a good read -- a detailed character study, a portrait of a family, a view into another world.

B000KQGOA4|4.0|Karajan's recording of Holst's The Planets with the Vienna Philharmonic from 1961 has been a favorite of collectors for decades. Indeed, the performance is quite good, although I do not agree with those who consider it the best ever The Planets. Karajan is at his best building pulse-pounding excitement in Mars, Bringer of War and also does a great job with the nightmarish scherzo that is Uranus. His Neptune is brisker than many, making for a more compelling listening experience. The two slower movements before Jupiter are well-played by the VPO and provide the necessary soothing after the brutish force of Karajan's Mars. Jupiter is a bit of a mix: the big tune is played triumphantly but it lacks the sentimentality of many other recordings, both British and non-British. The second theme of the movement (which precedes the big tune and makes a reprise toward the end) is taken too fast, as Gramophone noted in its review of the original LP. It's not terrible but it does detract from the coherence of this movement. Saturn too is a mix: it is loud and exciting at times but taken too fast and missing the mystery that the greatest performances have. What are they? Well, only one qualifies for near-perfection: Dutoit/Montreal, also on Decca, recorded in 1986. Dutoit reveals all layers of mystery in this suite. His Mars is not quite as brutal as Karajan's but it's pretty powerful nonetheless. Dutoit's Jupiter is absolutely perfect: spot-on tempos, sentimental but not schmaltzy, a thrilling climax that doesn't unnaturally speed up toward the end of the movement, like Karajan and especially Mehta (with the LAPO, from 1971, also Decca) do. Dutoit's Saturn, taken at nearly a minute and a half longer than Karajan, reveals the power and mystery of the movement like no other performance. There are other great performances, especially those by Boult (for sound considerations, try his 1979 recording), but Dutoit is the best of them all.     The main reason for writing this review, though, isn't to point out any flaws in Karajan's execution since, at the end of the day, Karajan's performance still stands as one of the greats. (Just don't let it be your only recording). The biggest issue is the 1961 sound quality: although remastered with 96/24 technology, it still sounds somewhat recessed and dry. The blaring brass which you know are there for the climax of Mars sound like they're kind of in the background. Interestingly, the strings fare better throughout this recording. What's the point of noting deficiencies in an early 1960s recording, you ask? Well, because there are other recordings from the same period that are far superior. Take anything from the Mercury Living Presence series and (to a lesser extent) the RCA Living Stereo series and you'll see how those sound much more full-bodied and warm than Karajan's.     As for Don Juan, I have no complaints: Karajan was probably the best Strauss conductor out there and this, one of about five recordings Karajan made of this piece, is as brash and romantic as you could ask for. The playing is full of gusto and the performance has a coherence sometimes lacking in this work. Even the sound is better than The Planets, despite being from a year earlier (1960). An easy first choice for the Strauss. But for The Planets, although this is a keeper, don't think you're getting the best of the best, since the performance and especially the sound, could be a lot better.

B001KOFH2G|5.0|Created by Joss Whedon (Buffy, Dollhouse), Firefly was a series on FOX that was prematurely cancelled in 2002.  Although 14 episodes of the science fiction adventure were produced, only 11 were aired.  The tale that Whedon had only begun to tell, was not only left hanging, but tossed to the curb like unwanted trash.  If not for Whedon's determination, and the support of a tremendously loyal fan base, Firefly would have gone the way of countless other cancelled shows, appreciated by a few, but soon forgotten and swept into the gutter of TV history.  Thankfully, on the road to obscurity, something truly amazing and wonderful happened, as the series gained a new audience when released on DVD.  Whedon was always determined to finish telling his story, and with the support of Universal Studios, completed a screenplay for the film that would become Serenity.<br /><br />Released in 2005, Serenity is a triumphant return for the crew of the Firefly, and a magnificent achievement on almost all levels.  The film reunites the entire principal cast from the series, and takes quantum leaps forward in the scope of the story, the level of action, and the quality of the special effects.  Within the limits of a feature film, Whedon addresses most of the questions that were left hanging in the TV series.  The movie retains much of the same spirit, but has a darker more deadly serious tone.  Despite budgetary and time constraints, all the critical elements are raised to completely new levels.<br /><br />The focus of the TV series was mostly on the day to day adventures of the crew attempting find jobs and eek out a living.  Serenity however, is mostly about the Alliance's quest to recapture the mysterious River Tam (Summer Glau).  Once a passenger on the spaceship Serenity, the waifish River's importance is tremendously elevated, and her role expanded to include some impressive fight scenes.  Her brother Simon (Sean Maher) also has a larger role, as the crew of Serenity is basically at war with the Alliance, who have sent The Operative (Chiwetel Ejiofor), to lead the attempt to recover River.  Veterans of the Unification War, Malcolm Reynolds (Nathan Fillion) and Zoe Washburne (Gina Torres) return to a wartime mode, and put their fighting skills to use.  Fillion also has several extended fight sequences.  Pilot Hoban `Wash' Washburne (Alan Tudyk) shines in the battle sequences in space.  Jayne Cobb (Adam Baldwin) remains a guy you can mostly count on in a fight, while ship's mechanic Kaylee Frye (Jewel Staite) is still a wiz with gadgets and a spunky fighter.  Unfortunately many scenes between Reynolds and Inara Serra (Morena Baccarin) ended up being cut.  Shepherd Book's (Ron Glass) appearance is brief, but very touching.<br /><br />The backstory of the mysterious Reavers is revealed, when the Serenity arrives at the ghost planet Miranda.  The Reavers play a key role in the climactic finish that ends with a final battle at the headquarters of Mr. Universe (David Krumholtz).  After the Serenity crash lands, the crew prepares for a battle to the death with the Reavers.  The gentlemanly and overly polite Operative, has proven to be a ruthless persecutor, killing anyone that has ever been a friend to the Serenity.  The final act is a montage of mayhem that begins with a battle in space, then features the crew battling the zombie-like Reavers, and sustaining major injuries.  Down below in the generator room, Mal is tangling with The Operative, and manages to leave him speechless.  With most everyone injured, River engages the enemy alone, in a bloody ballet of death.<br /><br />The special effects heavy Serenity, looks absolutely fabulous on Blu-ray.  The special features are prodigious, with several exclusive to the 2008 Blu-ray.  Joss Whedon's presence is extensive, as he provides a solo commentary, and one with members of the cast recorded in 2007, that can also be viewed as inserted video onscreen.  Whedon discusses why the deleted scenes were cut, as well as many of the other decisions he made regarding the plot, and why not all the characters survive.<br />With Whedon's focus clearly on telling a story galactic in scope, certain sacrifices had to be made, and Serenity is mostly a tale of war, with scant time for romance.  While the film may not totally satisfy the desires of all hardcore fans, it does a very good job of covering most of the high points, and winding up most of the loose ends, as Whedon was not counting on doing another sequel.  As over five years have passed, it seems that he made a wise choice.  Predictably, the cast are all fabulous, particularly Nathan Fillion (Castle) and Summer Glau (Terminator: The Sara Connor Chronicles).  Serenity is a straight ahead action/adventure where the nuances included for the fans, still allow for it to be enjoyed by those who have never seen Firefly.  It's science fiction that is done right, not dumbed down for the masses.ny of the other decisions he made regarding the plot, and why not all the characters survive. Eventually, I was able to get over the painful rejection and move on.<br /><br />Eight years have passed and I realize that she made the right decision for the both of us.  In hindsight, I realize that she was not a very nice individual, and I would have been very unhappy if we were still together. So I am thankful.<br /><br />The reason why I'm starting of this review with a personal story is that 'A Discovery of Witches' reminds me of what I went through personally.  The protagonist, Diana Bishop, is a smart and hardworking professor of history at Oxford University.  She has multiple college degrees, has written books, and is  blessed with the supernatural powers of witch craft.  This woman has a lot going for her. Such a character has the potential to be a effective heroine in a novel. Sadly, she is not. In fact, she is an adult Bella Swan; an emo chick who pines over a beautiful male vampire named Matthew Claremont.<br /><br />The book had a lot of potential. Mixing history with themes of the supernatural is an intriguing premise. However, the author devotes a substantial amount of time on the unnecessary. There are entire paragraphs that detail Diana's choice of outfit; and Diana's obsession over Matthew is meticulously discussed. The author seems to take joy in writing about the superficial and the mundane. It's gotten to the point where I can only read a few pages a day because I can't stand the fact that I'm reading about nothing in particular.<br /><br />There is so much material in this novel that should not have been included; and there is so much material that the novel needed that is left out. I was hoping that the novel would enthrall me with adventure, magic, and history. Instead, I'm horrified because I've used my hard earned money to purchase a vanilla Daniel Steele novel, and I want my money back.<br /><br />Do not spend your money on 'A Discovery of Witches'. If you are really curious, wait until the novel is available at your neighborhood library. That way, you can read the novel for free and experience the disappointment without feeling like you've been swindled. If you want to read a book about a woman who loves a vampire, then read 'Twilight'. B00006I53S|4.0|I've had this lens for about 3 months now, and have taken 3000-3500 photos with it, mainly outside shots.  Most of the photos were shots of head stones in various cemetery's for my wife's volunteer genealogy work (Google findagrave if you are interested in finding out more).  Taking pictures of head stones sounds easy (they don't move very much ;)) but the composition of the various materials, their reflective properties and the aging of the stone's surface all combine to keep it interesting. In addition, many of the older cemetery's are in sad shape, with small trees and brush growing up around the stones. Several shots may be required for a given stone: shot of the stone itself, up-close shots of the text so my wife can transcribe the information later and associated nearby stones that might share the same relationship. When I first started taking cemetery shots, I was using a Canon EF 50mm f1.4 USM, which produced nice clear photos, but being a prime lens I was constantly moving to and fro acting as the telephoto component.  This was do-able for awhile, but after a few hundred shots with my legs acting as the telephoto, I started using the Canon EF 28-135mm f3.5-5.6 IS USM lens instead. I can shot a 5' tall stone and be within 6.5' of it, I could never do that with my 50mm lens.  That, and the ability of the telephoto to get a shot of a stone buried in some thorny brambles has made this lens indispensable when doing this type of work. In summary I enjoy using this lens, and it is quickly becoming my most used all purpose lens.  The lens does tend to extend out when carrying it around on your camera, and some people have mentioned the weight of the lens, neither of these are a concern with me, and I have walk for hours carrying my Canon T2i with this lens attached.

0812971825|5.0|Reading Elizabeth Strout's \\"Abide with Me\\" reminded me how fiction can sometime capture the truth of things better than a factual account, just as a fine painting can sometimes be more truthful than a photograph of the same scene.<br /><br />I heard Strout speak a few years ago at a Bangor Theological Seminary Convocation, and I knew her book was about a congregational minister in rural Maine, but I only just got around to reading it.  I'm glad I did.<br /><br />The resonances for me to my own life are striking.  I am not Tyler Caskey, her protagonist, but I did begin my ministry in a couple of very small rural Maine towns that bear a notable resemblance to the fictional West Annett.  And I left those congregations to become the chaplain at Bangor Seminary, which is the model for Tyler's alma mater, Brockmorton Theological Seminary (a whimsical reference I am sure to my late former colleague, iconic Bangor New Testament Professor Burton H. Throckmorton.)<br /><br />Like Tyler I married a Massachusetts gal who came up to live with me in the parsonage to much speculation.  There are many differences to be sure:  I started my ministry in the mid 70's and Tyler in the late 50's, but things in small town Maine hadn't changed all that much.<br /><br />Stout deftly describes the \\"wheels within wheels\\" complexity behind the seemingly simple social life of a small Maine town.  The people of West Annett endure the soul-numbing endless winter, and they are unaware of how they have embraced their dearth of possibilities as a virtue.<br /><br />Strout takes her time. You know from the first page that some bad things have happened to Tyler Caskey and the denizens of West Annett, but she is no hurry to tell you what they are.  Her storytelling is like peeling an onion, and that in itself captures the rhythm of these small towns, where nothing ever seems to happen on the surface when it is really as busy as an ant farm just below.<br /><br />Tyler himself is a loveable character, too earnest by half, with his love of Bonhoeffer, his tenderness toward is wounded young daughter, and his quiet faithfulness in his daily round. Strout knows her church, and she knows something of the grandeur and misery of the ministry, as the minister can move in a minute from reading the Cost of Discipleship to hearing tawdry local gossip or the sordid confession of a soured marriage.  Her cast of characters will bring a smile to many a rural parson:  the hostile husband reading the paper in the car in the church parking lot, the loyalist who routinely phones Tyler to warn him what's up,  several variants of antagonists, and the married woman with a crush on the minister as well as a bone to pick.<br /><br />Strout observes her characters with clear eyes, and her depictions at times just miss being cruel. If you care for these flawed people at all it is because of something like grace, since they are not \\"good\\" people in the way that real people  generally are not.  Yet in the end, in keeping with its subject matter, this is a story of redemption.  Strout doesn't clean up the messiness of life, but she knows that the holy rhythm that runs from Good Friday to Easter isn't confined to ancient Jerusalem.<br /><br />I don't want to give too much away.  Read Abide with Me.  It's the kind of book that when you finish the last page and close the cover you are already missing the characters.<br /><br />[...]

Although me suggesting technical accuracy is kind of like a first grader telling a college professor that their solution looks good (me being the first grader).  We always think of virus' as very specific in nature to target a specific operating system and/or software application so to start in on this book and quickly realize this virus infects a very diverse amount of equipment is very interesting.  I get the strong feeling that Mark wrote this book as a venue to openly explain very tangible possibilities, regarding the public and private efforts on both perspectives (hackers and cybercrime institutions) while being able to firmly stand behind the veil that he wrote a science fiction not a non-fiction.  He is putting the world on notice, plain and simple.  This really isn't science fiction, more like a futurist perspective salted with characters to make the read easier to chew on.  Many times in the book Mark brings \\"facts and statistics\\" forward which immediately made me stop reading for a sec, stare at the wall and mutter \\"holy s...., not good\\".  From this perspective I strongly recommend you understand and/or research Mark's career.  He pulled the pants down on both Sony and Symantec for embedding root kits in their software/hardware.  This is mentioned in the book with no reference to himself as this is science fiction .. . .right?  So, you get the idea real fast, that this scenario is not fabricated like most \\"save the planet\\" jokes of sci-fi are.  The book is even more riveting when you know this isn't just another author having done homework on the topic.  In Wikipedia under career the last sentence reads, \\"He is widely regarded as a Windows expert.\\"  Negative; he is widely regarded as the person experts turn to when they are at a loss.  Don't know what that label is, but that about sums it up.  One final note.  If you're not in the computer world or think this book is above your capability to understand, don't let that hold you up.  The 'meat' of the book is based around the situations and consequence of a computer driven world and he does a great job keeping you engaged and thinking how things could possibly get worse and hopefully get better.  You don't need to have any amazing computer sk1llz to engage.      Just start the damn thing on a 'Friday' so you don't spend all Tuesday night reading and have to go to work the next day; like I did!
