0525423273|4.0|Okay, first, let me address something that has been bugging me about reviews for this book: Stephanie Perkin's debut novel is not a chick lit. I repeat, it is not chick lit. It's a romance. There is a difference. Chick lit is basically about women (typically in their 20s, professional, and single) and how they relate to themselves, other women, and men. Romance may be a part of the story but isn't necessary. A romance novel, on the other hand, is centered around a romance [obviously]. Chick lit books focus mainly on how the female protag changes and the broader conflict of her vs. the world, as opposed to her vs. a man, like a romance. Therefore, Anna and the French Kiss is a romance.    Whew! Now that that's over...    I really, really, really wanted to love this book. I enjoy a good contemporary every once in a while. I had seen so many outstanding reviews that I thought it would be impossible for me not to love it. But honestly, it didn't wow me. *ignoring audible gasps from readers*    I've got to tell you, Anna's griping about her dad was obnoxious. She is so terribly embarrassed of him and for no honestly good reason. Heaven forbid he make money. Heaven forbid he try to better her life by sending her to a good school. What was she so angry about? I could understand missing your family and being miffed that your father wanted to take you away from your friends and school senior year of high school, but I think mostly everyone I've ever met would have jumped at the chance to live in Paris.    Aside from Anna's annoying tendency to be slightly bratty, I enjoyed her character. I also really liked St. Clair and all the other friends she meets. They were well-developed, and I loved their interactions. I also liked that St. Clair wasn't tall, dark, and handsome. He was an average bloke (hehe) and had flaws: he bit his fingernails constantly, he was really short, and he was terrified of heights. That all made him interestings, so props to Perkins for that!    I also liked how the relationship was slow and progressed throughout the story. There was no love-at-first-sight. Sure, there was attraction, but that's legit. You can be attracted to someone the second you meet them, but odds are, you don't love them. (Take notes, Shakespeare!) This was also a pretty squeaky clean romance. Sure, Anna thinks about sex. (All teens do.) But she states outright that she's not ready for it, and she even admits to being a virgin. I liked that. She knew who she was and didn't feel the pressure to conform. Plus, I'm a sucker for a romance that doesn't actually happen until the end (reminds me of Jane Austen), so I really liked that about this book, as well.    Speaking of the end, herein lies my chief complaint. And honestly, I think this all boils down to personal preference. I have a hard time with happy endings. If someone doesn't die, the book just doesn't feel complete. Anna and the French Kiss is sugary sweet - a little too much so for me. Everything is tied up pretty with a bow on top at the end, and I wanted some gut-wrenching pain that makes you put the book down and want to cry your eyes out but that you know had to end that way because it was just right. This could even be a happy ending book, but there needed to be some suffering along the way.    I was told once that all great literature has one overarching theme: human suffering. That was what was lacking in Anna for me: good old fashion pain.    Everything is cute and adorable, but that's not really my cup of tea...unless it's a Sophie Kinsella or Lauren Willig novel, in which case I don't care that it's corny happy because I'm laughing so hard I'm snorting. Maybe that's what I was missing in Anna: humor. I didn't get any. Sure, it was cute...but that was it. Fluff. There's certainly nothing wrong with fluff, but I guess I just wanted there to be more sustenance.    I picked out exactly where I would have liked the book to end: page 347, after Anna has almost \\"ruined\\" her whole life. She's at the theater:    \\"And I realize...it's okay. It's okay if St. Clair and I never become more than friends. His friendship alone has strengthened me in a way that no one else's ever has. He swept me from my room and showed me independence. In other words, he was exactly what I needed.\\"    And...end book. There would have been such a strong message here, that even though life doesn't always turn out the way we want it to, it's for the best, that the people we meet like St. Clair might not end up being our besties or our boyfriends but they change who we are, and we're better people for it. ...not \\"and they lived happily ever after.\\"

0865479186|5.0|Even though the title indicates a generic topic \\"Bad Science\\", be aware this book deals almost exclusively with medical topics. It's entertaining and informative.  The author's target audience is a generally intelligent layman, and with that target audience he has done a masterful job.  It should be required reading for everyone who is interested in medicine, nutrition, and their health.  It does not contain advanced material, but that is not its purpose and so it shouldn't be criticized for that.  You can't be all things to all people or you will be nothing to anyone.    The author's style is a little bit confrontational, but he does it with such wit it works perfectly.  He does a very effective job of skewering the purveyors of pseudo-science and their co-conspirators in the credulous media.  Here is the author's own description of his book in the preface:    \\"It's easy to laugh at quacks - but this book is not about easy targets or individuals.  It follows a natural crescendo, from the foolishness of quacks, via the credence they are given in the mainstream media, through the tricks of the fifty-five-billion-dollar food supplements industry, the evils of the six-hundred-billion-dollar pharmaceuticals industry, the tragedy of science reporting, and on to cases where people have wound up in prison, derided, or dead, simply through the poor understanding of statistics and evidence that pervades our society.\\"    Very highly recommended.

0385732554|3.0|I felt obligated to read The Giver, since I'd heard so much about it from friends who teach middle school English, I'd read about it in a number of scholarly articles about Young Adult literature, and it seems to be rising to the level of a YA classic. It's relatively brief, and it's a very quick read, and it seems almost purposely designed to be taught as a middle school YA novel. The futuristic setting in which everyone exists under a doctrine of Sameness in a socially engineered society and children's future occupations are determined for them at the age of 12 by a council of Elders, along with the absence of real emotions, colors, and memories clearly telegraphs a number of \\"Important Themes\\" that provide very rich fodder for trenchant analysis in middle school literature classrooms. Now, I'm not denying the value of this novel as a staple of 7th and 8th grade literature curricula--if I were still teaching secondary English, I could certainly see myself including this in my school's curriculum. In my opinion, however, the best YA novels are--first and foremost--good novels that will also appeal to young adults (e.g., any of the Harry Potter novels). Genre fiction definitely has its place, but I suspect that most young adults who read this novel read it because it's assigned to them in class and not because it has any kind of intrinsic appeal.

B0000C8AW7|5.0|I own every album Dylan has released except for a few of his less highly-regarded live ones such as \\"Dylan and The Dead.\\" This is easily in his top ten if not top five, though the die-hard fossilized hippie types may dismiss it (as they tend to do with anything else he did after 1969 except Blood On the Tracks.)<br /><br />His voice is rough but expressive, and all of the songs strike me as either highly entertaining or downright great. The lyrics lack the surrealism of the earlier \\"greats\\" such as Blonde on Blonde or Highway 61, but have a more incisive realism, full of pithy observations, humorous asides, and highly evocative, colorful phraseology.<br /><br />The whole feel of the album is very \\"southern,\\" seeming to speak in voices of old plantation hands, washed-up riverboat gamblers, retired sharecroppers, former Confederate soldiers, small-town romantics, gator-swamp rats, and the like. None too overtly, though - it's a more subtle kind of \\"feel\\" to the atmosphere than anything identifiably obvious. Even the instruments strike me as \\"southern sounding,\\" though I can't describe why. It all tends to sound like an old folk-philosopher sitting on his front porch at sunset, dispensing rough gems of worldly wisdom and telling the occasional corny joke. Only the first song really avoids this feel, being a quick-paced and more surreal commentary on some ambiguous pair of losers referred to as Tweedle Dum and Tweedle Dee.<br /><br />Also, the album is overall quite upbeat, unlike \\"Time Out of Mind.\\" I feel that it is the best of his last five albums, though Time Out of Mind is close and Modern Times isn't bad either.

0449000729|4.0|To call this 900+ tome a saga is selling it short because this ambition undertaking by Rutherford encompasses the history -- from the last iceage, Michener-esque style -- right up to the 1980s of present day Salisbury, England.  Generations of the same handful of fictional families are traced and through them, Rutherford gives what is perhaps the most comprehensive history of this area that includes ancient Stonehenge.  Not for the faint of heart.  But well worth the read, especially if you plan on visiting the area.  His characterizations are wonderful and his history detailed.  And don't expect a family-saga just because the same families are traced through the years....frequently family lines disappear, only to reappear multiple generations later.  And whatever you do, don't expect a fairy tale ending....Rutherford is good with plot twists.  The novel is definitely an investment of your time.  No skimming allowed in this one or you'll lose the thread of the story.  Take your time, read it slowly and enjoy.
